1. INTRODUCTION
A. OVERVIEW OF THE ENERGY SECTOR
Supply and Demand
1.1   China has become the third largest producer of energy in the world. The
country's primary commercial energy supply and consumption increased at around 4.1
percent per year from 1980 to 1995 to reach 1,239.0 tons of standard coal equivalent
(tce). Coal is the most important source of energy, accounting for about 74.8 percent, and
oil for 17.2 percent of the total production. Hydroelectric power (6.1 percent), natural gas
(1.9 percent), and small quantities of shale oil and geothermal power make up the
balance. During the same period, the primary energy consumption increased at about 5.1
percent to reach 1,290.0 tce. The largest consumer of energy is the industrial sector (75.4
percent), followed by households (9.7 percent), services (8.6 percent), and agriculture
(6.3 percent). Details of the primary energy outputs are given in Annex 1.1, and total
energy production and consumption in Annex 1.2.
Energy Resources
1.2   Coal. China has large coal deposits, with recoverable reserves of about 900
billion tons, of which 30 percent are proven. In 1995, the country produced more than
1.2 billion tons of raw coal. By the year 2000, China aims to produce 1.4 to 1.6 billion
tons of coal a year. The best quality coals are found in North China, where Shanxi and
Inner Mongolia each has reserves of about 200 billion tons. However, insufficient
transport capacity makes it extremely difficult to move sufficient coal to the large
consumption centers in Central and East China.
1.3   Hydroelectric Potential. China is rich in water resources and has a long tradition
of harnessing them for energy and other uses. The country's hydroelectric potential is
estimated at 1,900 TWh a year, but only about 9 percent of it has been developed. Most
of the potential is located in the southwest (70 percent), about 1,500 km away from the
main demand centers. However, the long gestation period for hydroelectric projects and
the lack of capital have slowed the development and utilization of hydroelectric
resources.
1.4   Crude Oil. China's ultimately recoverable reserves of crude oil are estimated at
some 80 billion tons, of which approximately 20 percent are proven. The 1995
production amounted to about 150 million tons of crude oil and cumulative production
reached about 2.45 billion tons. China's refining capacity is the sixth largest in the
world. However, with the sharply increasing consumption, the country will become net
importer in the near future.



-2 -
1.5   Natural Gas. China's natural gas resources are estimated at 33 trillion m3, of
which 3 to 5 percent are proven. In 1995, China produced 17.7 billion m3 of natural gas.
About 58 percent of the current production is nonassociated gas, mostly from Sichuan
Province. The remaining gas production is associated with onshore crude oil production.
1.6   Biomass. Noncommercial biomass energy use currently amounts to about one
quarter of total energy consumption in China. Fuelwood and agricultural residues are the
chief biomass fuels, and they are consumed almost entirely by rural households. China is
promoting a variety of measures to achieve environmentally sustainable biomass supply
and consumption levels, including more efficient use of biomass, tree planting, and
substitution of other fuels for traditional biomass fuels.
1.7   Nuclear Energy. Known uranium reserves in China are sufficient to sustain
15,000 MW of nuclear power capacity for 30 years. China commissioned its first
commercial nuclear power plant (300 MW) in 1992, in Zhejiang Province. In 1993/94,
the Daya Bay nuclear power plant (2x900 MW) was also commissioned; 70 percent of its
output is intended for nearby Hong Kong. The construction of a few additional plants is
planned to start before the year 2000, primarily in regions which lack coal and
hydroelectric resources.
1.8   Other Energy Resources. Other energy resources play a small role in energy
supply and are used mostly in remote areas. Geothermal energy has been found in
locations throughout the country. Although wind and solar energy have promising
prospects, they are not expected to significantly affect the overall supply of energy in the
near future. Oil shale deposits are large, but little is exploited because of lack of viable
technology.
B. THE POWER SECTOR
Supply and Demand
1.9   China is the second largest producer of electricity in the world. By the end of
1995, China's total installed capacity reached about 217.2 GW, 75 percent of which is
thermal capacity and the rest hydropower (24 percent) and nuclear power (1 percent)
(Annex 1.3). China has five regional and eight separate provincial power networks, of
which seven have installed capacities of 10 GW or more. The five regional power grids
(East China, North China, Northeast China, Central China and Northwest China)
coordinate the systems of several provinces and municipalities together. The total length
of the high and medium-voltage transmission lines (i.e., from 500 kV down to 110 kV)
lines amounted to 267,500 km and the transformer capacity of substations down to the
110 kV at the secondary level (owned and operated by the power utilities) was about
234 GVA. Considering the power supply as a whole from production to end-user supply,
transmission and distribution losses are estimated at about 16 percent of generation.
1.10  Total power generation in China increased by more than two and one-half times
between 1980 and 1995, an average increase of 8.0 percent per year over 14 years. About



- 3 -
1,007 TWh were generated in 1995, with thermal power accounting for 80 percent. The
share of hydroelectric power has remained fairly stable over the last decade. Even though
the Government has maintained a strong commitment to hydropower development,
capital constraints and the distance of major resources from load centers inhibit more
rapid development. Coal-fired power plants provide more than 90 percent of thermal
generation, with oil-fired generation accounting for most of the balance. The share of oil-
fired generation has declined sharply since the early 1980s, owing to a concerted
government policy to minimize petroleum use for power. The share of natural-gas based
generation is negligible and is expected to remain so unless major natural gas discoveries
are made.
1.11  Industry continues to dominate electricity consumption in China, accounting for
74.9 percent of electricity use in 1995; agriculture accounted for 6.2 percent, residential
consumers for 10.2 percent, municipal and commercial consumers for 6.9 percent, and
transport and communications for 1.8 (Annex 1.4). Industrial demand has continued to
drive overall power demand; more than 71.5 percent of incremental electricity use
between 1985 and 1995 was in the industrial sector. Although service is of uneven
quality, rural access to electricity is high; about 96 percent of the nation's villages and
about 80 percent of rural families now have access to electricity.
1.12  Despite the strong growth in electricity output, most areas of China continue to
suffer from severe power shortages. Since GDP grew by 9.3 percent per year during
1980-95, the elasticity of electricity consumption growth relative to GDP growth was
only 0.86-an exceptionally low elasticity for any country over a sustained period, and
particularly for an industrializing country such as China. Chinese officials estimate the
shortfall between peak power demand and supply at more than 10 percent. Daily load
curves are relatively flat, as industries have had to respond to power shortages during the
peak daytime periods through rescheduling of work shifts. It is expected that power
shortages will be more acute in the two to three coming years because of accumulated
delays in power projects due to financial constraints.
1.13  China's GDP is expected to grow by at least 8 to 9 percent per year during the
ninth five-year plan (1996-2000). This rapid economic growth will put tremendous
pressure on China's electric power industry to avoid yet worse shortages. China's
electric power construction program for the 1990s will certainly be the world's largest.
Assuming that an aggressive conservation effort can enable an electricity demand/GDP
growth elasticity of 1.0 to be sustained, electricity demand could be expected to continue
to grow at average rates of 8 to 9 percent per year during 1996-2000. To keep pace with
this demand growth, China would need to add some 17-20 GW of capacity per year. The
total investment in China in 1995 was Y 1,945 billion, of which Y 101 billion (5.2
percent) was in the power sector.
1.14  Although China's current power development program emphasizes addition of
300 to 600 MW units, over one half of China's thermal power capacity is still in units
below 200 MW. Only about 12 percent of the total installed capacity is in units of



- 4 -
300 MW or more, compared to 60 to 80 percent in industrialized countries. The average
gross conversion efficiency of thermal units over 6 MW has improved over the 1980s,
reaching about 33 percent in 1995. This efficiency level is not unreasonable given the
low average unit sizes in operation, but is substantially lower than the 36 to 37 percent
typical of reasonably efficient systems with larger units.
Power Sector Institutions
1.15 In the period 1988-92, the Ministry of Energy (MOE) maintained primary
responsibility within the Central Government for policy, planning and regulation of the
power and related energy subsectors. In May 1993, MOE was restructured during a
broad government reorganization, and a Ministry of Electric Power (MOEP) was
established, along with a Ministry of Coal and the existing China National Petroleum
Corporation. Chart 1 shows the entities that are within MOEP oversight. The State
Planning Commission (SPC), on behalf of the State Council, has the responsibility for
review and approval of the strategic plans, investment programs and general pricing
policy of the energy sector. The Ministry of Water Resources still supervises rural
electrification and development of decentralized power systems in approximately 800
counties relying mostly on small hydropower.
1.16  Until early 1993, the State Energy Investment Corporation (SEIC) channeled
Government funds to finance coal and power projects. SEIC was responsible for
onlending funds for projects of national importance and representing the central
Government in jointly financed projects. However, in April 1994, SEIC (and five other
specialized investment companies under SPC) were merged into a new policy bank, the
State Development Bank (SDB). The Construction Bank of China usually performs
policy lending as a disbursement/collection agency for SDB, and commercial banking
activities on its own account. For provincial power companies, equity and loans/debt
representing central government's contribution are channeled through the regional
groups. In some cases, such as Ertan Hydropower Electric Company Ltd., equity stakes
are held by the State Development Investment Corporation (SDIC).
C. POWER SECTOR REFORMS-PROGRESS AND FUTURE DIRECTIONS
1.17  Reforms in China's power sector during the 1980s involved a series of
incremental adjustments, largely to mobilize additional investment capital to finance
rapid growth. Taken as a whole, these adjustments have resulted in a sector structure that
is largely unrecognizable from that in the early 1980s. In 1980, the power sector was
fully controlled, managed and funded by Central Government departments. By the early
1990s, the sector had become much more decentralized, less vertically integrated and
open to nonutility producers.
1.18  In conformance with broad national reforms toward a market economy begun in
mid-1992, the Government initiated, in the early 1990s, a program of power sector
reforms. The reform agenda includes: (a) overhaul of the regulatory and legal
framework for the sector; (b) further diversification in financing for power development,



-5-
including private sector participation; (c) institutional restructuring-commercialization
and corporatization of power companies, and associated sector restructuring; and (d) the
rationalization of power tariffs.  The Chinese approach to reform  encourages
experimentation with different reform options and institutional forms. This gradual
approach permits the Chinese to examine the relevance and applicability of specific
reforms, and also to fine-tune implementation for their broader dissemination or
replication in other parts of the country.
1.19 Legal and Regulatory Reform. China recently undertook two important steps in
its legal and regulatory reform agenda. First, the Standing Committee of the 17th session
of the National People's Congress passed the first "Electricity Law of the People's
Republic of China," which became effective on April 1, 1996 (Box 1.1). Second, the
State Council (a) approved the creation of the National Power Grid Company, which will
operate the future national (interregional) transmission network; and (b) proposed for
consideration by the National People's Congress a restructuring plan that provides for
abolition of MOEP and transfer of regulatory and policy functions to the State Economic
and Trade Commission (SETC), the State Planning Commission (SPC) and the China
Electricity Council. A State Power Company has been created and entrusted with the
ownership functions of the central government and development of interregional
transmission systems.
1.20 An important and innovative experiment in regulatory reform is being conducted
in Hainan Province to make the transition to an unbundled sector structure with separate
regulation.  Actions undertaken include:  creation of a single transmission and
distribution shareholding company; several generating companies, including a wholly
foreign-owned one; and establishment on June 1, 1995 of a separate agency to regulate
the sector. Lessons learned from this experiment will be taken into account in furthering
the power sector reform efforts in other provinces. A more ambitious experiment is
under consideration for Fujian Province.
1.21  Diversification of Financing. Following changes in power project financing
from Government grants to loans in the early 1980s, the Government encouraged the
development of additional financing sources from provincial and local governments,
large consumers, and, in some cases, private foreign partners. Since the mid-1980s, the
Government has allowed power produced from power plants financed through these
nontraditional sources to be sold at prices which provide for full debt servicing and
reasonable profit. This is referred to as the "new plant, new price policy." Empowered
with rights to impose surcharges to accumulate funds for investment in power
construction, provincial and local governments have become a key source of financing,
providing more than half of the investment funding for the sector in 1995. As a
consequence, the share of Central Government financing in power sector investment has
fallen sharply, from 91 percent (mostly through State Budget allocations) in 1980 to less
than 35 percent in 1995.



- 6 -
Box 1.1: THE ELECTRIC POWER LAW FOR THE PEOPLE'S REPUBLIC OF CHINA
On December 28, 1995, the Standing Committee of the National People's Congress enacted a
comprehensive new law that applies to every part of the power sector. The Electric Power Law became
effective April 1996, and is intended to support reforms necessary to introduce the socialist market
economy to the power sector.
The Electric Power Law generally specifies broad principles that must be implemented through the
issuance of additional State Council regulations and other legal instruments. The Electric Power Law
provides a solid foundation upon which to establish an adequate regulatory system. However, consistent
with the direction of reforms for the Chinese economy, the Electric Power Law has the following specific
objectives:
.  To encourage the development of the power industry consistent with development of a socialist
market economy.
* To encourage the development of poor and rural areas.
* To encourage private investment in power generation, but not in other power sector activities,
such as transmission and distribution.
. To protect the legal interests of power enterprises, investors, and power customers.
* To encourage the development of renewable resources and new technology.
* To protect the safety of power facilities and installations.
The Electric Power Law covers the construction of power facilities and the supply of power in China and
applies to both Chinese and foreign companies. The Law includes 10 chapters and covers, among other
things, electric power construction, electric power and electric network management, supply and utilization
of power, power tariffs, rural electric power construction and agricultural use of power, and the protection
of electric facilities.
The Electric Power Law includes several provisions directly relevant to the design and creation of the legal
framework for the power sector, including the following:
* Article 6 sets forth the government institutions responsible for regulation of the power sector.
* Article 7 guarantees power enterprises' rights to operate autonomously subject to government
supervision.
* Articles 10 through 12 set forth requirements governing power sector planning.
* Articles 18 through 23 set forth requirements for power production and electric network system
service.
* Article 25 requires issuance of power supply licenses to serve customers within exclusive
geographic service territories.
* Articles 26 through 34 set forth the service requirements that must be satisfied by power supply
enterprises and the specific rights and obligations of customers.
* Articles 25 through 35 set forth principles that govern tariff determinations and that allocate tariff
responsibilities among different levels within the government.
* Articles 56 through 58 authorize inspections for the purposes of enforcement of legal and
regulatory requirements.
* Articles 59 through 74 set forth penalties for violations of various provisions of the Electric
Power Law.
1.22 Newly formed corporations have also become important players. The Huaneng
International Power Development Corporation (HIPDC) was formed in 1985 with the aim



of attracting foreign capital and technology for power plants located mainly in coastal
areas. As an independent power producer, HIPDC operates its 11 plants (6,700 MW
total) under a build, own and operate (BOO) framework. HIPDC had a mandate to raise
funds from the international financial market. It has been able to tap bilateral and export
credits, and commercial bank loans, and to raise foreign equity capital through an
American Depository Receipt (ADR) issue in New York in 1994. The Sunburst Energy
Development Corporation (SEDC), with three large power projects under
implementation, and the recently established China Power Investment Corporation
(CPIC) are other windows for the Government's investment, and cofinancing with the
local governments and foreign sources in power projects. Several joint ventures have
been established to build several power plants with capacity ranging from 40 MW to
700 MW. Two major joint venture projects have been recently approved in Shandong
Province (Rizhao power plant-2x350 MW coal-fired units) and in Shanghai (Zhadian
power plant-4x 100 MW gas turbines).
1.23  Build, own and transfer (BOT) schemes have also been introduced in China, most
notably the privately-financed Shajiao B and Shajiao C thermal power plants in
Guangdong Province, respectively commissioned in 1986 and 1996. Another major BOT
project sponsored by two international consortia have been approved by SPC in 1993 in
Fujian Province (Meizhou Wan power plant-2x350 MW coal-fired units). The process
of international competitive tendering for projects has also begun. SPC approved the Lai
Bin B project in the Guangxi Autonomous Region to be the first power project to pilot
the international competitive bidding framework. SPC invited 12 of the 31 companies/
consortia that sought prequalification to bid competitively for the Lai Bin B project. Six
international consortia participated in the competition. Bids were opened in May 1996
and an 18-year concession was awarded to the winning consortium on November 11,
1996. Bids will shortly be issued for Changsha project in Hunan Province.
D. POWER PRICING
1.24  Electricity price reform in China has involved a series of incremental changes
over the last 10 years. In the mid-1980s, multiple-track pricing was introduced for both
producers and consumers, in line with the shift away from full vertical integration in the
industry. This strategy was successful in increasing overall tariff levels but, by the end of
the decade, the power pricing system had become too complex and difficult to manage.
Recent reforms have therefore focused on simplification and rationalization of the
system.
1.25  At the producer level, the state catalog prices charged for power produced by
Central Government-financed plants were modified in 1993. Prices for the electricity
produced from all new power plants are now set by contract to cover financing and
operating costs (guidance prices), on a plant-by-plant basis, and rolled into the average
power tariff. Cost recovery through the tariff system for new investments is thus ensured.
Prices also are similarly determined for existing plants financed through loans. The
electricity tariff applied for plants built with grant financing before 1985 has been



- 8 -
rationalized to now include an improved mechanism to automatically adjust prices
annually to reflect fuel and transportation cost variations. Following the enactment of the
Electricity Law, the dialogue between the Chinese government and the Bank focused on
implementation of the new pricing principles to ensure that producer prices reflect the
level and structure of the cost supply (introduction of a capacity and energy payment
formula) and are conducive to economic dispatch and optimum production efficiency.
1.26  At the consumer level, the prices are in general an average of the state catalog
prices and the guidance prices (that reflect full costs of supply from sources not financed
through the Central Government) plus national and local fees and surcharges. While the
situation varies between regions, the nature of the existing pricing system is such that
average consumer prices (including fees and surcharges) approximate long-run marginal
cost levels in areas with rapid load growth, such as the coastal provinces. However, the
average prices in some of these provinces increased at a slower pace than the long-run
marginal cost due to limitation of price increases to control inflation in 1995 and 1996.
The average consumer price in these areas still covers more than 85 percent of the long-
run marginal cost. Prices in underdeveloped areas, such as Sichuan and Henan, are still
below long-run marginal cost levels, but are being gradually rationalized with increasing
supply from new plants.
1.27  While consumer tariffs, are approaching satisfactory levels, their structure remains
too complex, lacks transparency, and is inequitable. In most areas, including some North
China systems, unfair "old" administered rates (based on catalog prices) and "new" rates
(including higher prices for new power) continue to be applied to different consumers
through a complex quota system.  Some consumers are protected from  high-cost
electricity supply charges and certain local surcharges because they still hold "in-plan"
quota allocations, but other new or small consumers must pay high prices well above
actual supply costs. Following the enactment of the Electricity Law, further and speedier
progress is expected on abolishing the two-track pricing system (unification of tariffs has
been reaffirmed by article 37 of the Electricity Law) and improving the tariff structure to
reflect the costs of supply of final consumers.
E. ELECTRICITY CONSERVATION AND ENVIRONMENTAL PROTECTION
Electricity Conservation
1.28  China has over 10 years of experience in the active promotion of electricity
conservation, with a solid record of achievement. The strength of its program lies in the
well-developed institutional framework. Electricity conservation is handled by the
network of "Three Electricity Offices," with offices at central, provincial, prefectural and
county levels throughout the country. These offices are under the leadership of local
Economic Commissions, and coordinate their work with provincial power companies.
They monitor electricity intensity and use patterns in all major enterprises, promote
adoption of conservation technologies and electricity management improvements, and
assist in organizing funding for efficiency programs supported by the Government.



1.29  These measures have been successful, as is indicated by the low ratio of elasticity
of growth in electricity demand to GDP growth of 0.86 achieved during 1980-95.
However, energy electricity conservation programs have always been based on centrally
designed energy/electricity conservation policies and heavily-subsidized administrative
programs. Faced with constrained fiscal revenues, the government is now promoting a
shift in the energy conservation programs to rely more on market-based incentives and
introduce innovative and commercially-based contractual and financing mechanisms.
Environmental Protection
1.30 Thermal power production currently accounts for about one third of China's coal
consumption, and this share is expected to increase gradually. Improvements in the
efficiency of coal use in this sector not only alleviate pressure on the coal production and
transportation system, but also have a major impact on particulate, sulfur and carbon
dioxide emissions.
1.31  The achievement of economies of scale in thermal power production, through
expanded development of large generating units, is a priority because of the recent rapid
growth of small coal-fired power plants, in unit sizes of 50 MW or less. While national
policy emphasizes the addition of 300 MW and 600 MW units, new projects have lagged
behind demand and local governments are continuing to invest in large numbers of new
small plants, largely due to difficulties in mobilizing the necessary investment resources.
Compared with a state-of-the-art plant, these plants consume over 60 percent more coal
per unit of electricity produced. This results in at least 60 percent higher sulfur dioxide
(SO2) emissions (depending upon the coal used), and roughly 60 percent greater
emissions of carbon dioxide.  Particulate emissions from  the small plants are
proportionally even higher, due to use of inefficient particulate control technology. If a
sufficient number of large-scale projects cannot be brought on stream in a timely fashion
to substitute smaller inefficient units, there is little doubt that this problem will worsen.
1.32 In terms of emission control in large- and medium-scale plants, China has made
substantial progress in particulate control, through deployment of high-efficiency
electrostatic precipitators. As the country has large reserves of low-sulfur coal, China is
just beginning to employ SO2 control devices in areas where ambient air quality standards
require them. Top priority should be given to areas that must rely on local high-sulfur
coal, such as in southwest China. In these regions, thermal power plants often bum coal
of over 4 percent sulfur content (e.g., 5 to 10 times the level typical through most of north
and east China), and development of SO2 control strategies and corresponding
investments are now urgent in these areas. Several pilot flue gas desulfurization (FGD)
projects have been implemented in areas where the sulfur content of coal is relatively
high: Luohang in Sichuan Province, Japanese-assisted projects in Shandong Province
and Taiyuan in Shanxi Province.
1.33  Recently, the Chinese Government initiated serious efforts to curb air pollution
related to the burning of coal and particularly to reduce the power sector's contribution to



- 10-
the problem. The Government also announced its intention to keep particulate emissions
at about 3.8 million tons (1992 level). It also announced in June 1994 that it will spend
about $2 billion over the next seven years on an environmental program aimed at keeping
SO2 emissions at a level of 15 million metric tons a year, as part of a comprehensive
program of acid rain abatement. An SO2 emission tax (from Y 0.15 to 0.2/kg of SO2) is
being experimented with in several provinces and municipalities and a tax (Y 0.04/kg of
SO2) is applied to emissions exceeding the 1982 standards in all provinces. Several
municipalities, such as Shanghai, issued very stringent environmental regulations and
increased efforts to enforce existing regulations by imposing fines on industrial units and
power plants that do not comply with emission standards and/or imposing use of flue gas
desulfiurization for all new plants.  However, achievement of the Government's
environmental objectives still requires a more adequate enforcement of the national
regulations.
F. ROLE OF THE WORLD BANK
World Bank Power Sector Assistance Strategy
1.34 In line with Bankwide policies, the Bank's energy assistance program in China
places special emphasis on sector reform and energy conservation. During the last two
years, the Bank's technical assistance program for the power sector has focused on
helping the Chinese in assessing reform options (through a study funded by the Bank's
Institutional Development Fund), defining implementation strategies (through sector
work on power sector reform), and exploring options for mobilizing domestic and foreign
private funds to meet the capital needs of sector development (through informal sector
work). The study (Strategic Options in Power Sector Reform in China, 1993) and sector
report (China Power Sector Reform: Toward Competition and Improved Performance,
Report 12929-CHA, 1994) provide vehicles for dialogue between the Bank and Chinese
institutions. Technical assistance is also now being provided to help the central
authorities develop an improved legal and regulatory framework for the sector. The
energy conservation support program includes technical assistance through the Global
Environment Facility (GEF), lending support to selected industrial, environrent and
energy projects, and development of a series of new Bank/GEF investment operations.
The power sector lending program now focuses on assistance for: (a) the detailed aspects
of implementation of the power sector reforms, (b) balanced development of generation
and transmission to reduce the power shortages and transmission bottlenecks that
continue to hamper development and slow the market orientation of the sector,
(c) increased efficiency at the supply and end-use levels, and (d) enhanced environmental
protection.
Bank Involvement
1.35  The proposed project is consistent with the Country Assistance Strategy (CAS)
for China presented to the Board on March 18, 1997. It supports the joint agenda of the
Chinese Government and the World Bank to improve the efficiency and minimize the



- 11 -
environmental impacts of electricity delivery and use while concurrently alleviating
existing power shortages and satisfying the surging demand. These objectives will be
achieved through (a) installation of larger generating units to take advantage of higher
fuel efficiency and economies of scale; (b) mine-mouth location of generating plants to
reduce the cost of coal delivered to the plant, and reduce overloading on the railway
system; (c) minimize action of pollution loads in densely populated areas with low local
absorptive capacity; and (d) introduction of market-based policies and competition
(where possible) and opening the sector further to domestic and foreign private investors.
1.36  Under the proposed project, the establishment of a limited liability company as an
independent power producer will significantly support commercialization of the sector,
and will pave the way for other independent power producers, including those owned by
private investors.  Following the successful approach of the Ertan Hydropower
Development Company, the Tuoketuo Electric Power Generation Company (TEPGC)
will be the first nonutility thermal power generator in China directly supported by the
Bank. The divestiture of generation assets, for which technical assistance is included in
the project, and the use of funds raised on international financial markets to finance
equity in TEPGC would be a significant step toward eliminating barriers to entry,
opening the power sector to private investors and introducing competition at the
generation level. The power purchase agreement can serve as a model for other IPPs and
also help develop North China Power Group Company's (NCPGC) role as a purchasing
agent and thereby stimulate unbundling of and power trade within the North China
system. Furthermore, improved energy efficiency at the generation, transmission and
distribution levels will reduce the adverse environmental impacts of electricity generation
and usage.
G. LESSONS LEARNED
1.37  Since the first project for the power sector in 1982, the Bank has helped to finance
18 large power generation and load management projects, eight thermal, eight hydro, and
two transmission projects (Annex 1.5). Technical assistance in project management,
system operations, finance, management and institutional reform has been included in the
projects. Through these operations, the Bank has helped to introduce: (a) state-of-art
technologies and control systems in generation, transmission, distribution and pollution
control; (b) modern operational practices and procedures to improve power system
reliability and control; (c) improved methods for project planning, design, construction
management, and environmental impact assessment; (d) improved financial planning and
utility management tools and practices; (e) techniques for computing and establishing
appropriate tariff structures and levels; and (f) assistance in sector and regulatory reform.
1.38  The past operations in China, especially the eight thermal power projects, yielded
useful lessons on project preparation, technical and operating aspects of power systems
and sequencing of institutional reform. The experience gained from these projects and
broader international experience in power sector restructuring have also been taken into



- 12 -
account in developing a power sector strategy to be pursued by the Bank in China, and in
designing focused and implementable institutional changes.
1.39  Lessons learned from the previous thermal power projects were considered during
preparation of the proposed project in order to: (a) improve the project management and
ensure proper coordination between the different contractors; (b) improve the quality of
the environmental studies, environmental management plans and monitoring measures;
and (c) ensure commitment of the beneficiary(ies) to the institutional and corporate
reforms to be included in the project. Actions have been taken to involve international
consultants in assisting the beneficiary(ies) to: (a) assess the environmental impacts of
the project and designing the proper measures to address them, (b) incorporate the new
generating company, and (c) prepare a power purchase agreement in line with
international practice. Finally, a serious boiler accident at the Beilungang power station
in Zhejiang province will be taken into account in boiler design and specifications, coal
and "check coal" specifications, and adequate measures of coal quality monitoring.
1.40  In power sector restructuring and reform implementation, international experience
shows that even in advanced countries with strong and transparent institutional systems
and well-established market economies, the time needed to build consensus on the reform
directions, establish a new regulatory framework, and implement structural changes can
be very long. China faces the same problems witnessed in Central and Eastern Europe.
Hopes for achieving quick progress in economic regulation and restructuring of the power
sectors in these countries have been unfulfilled because of the intricate links of
institutional changes to politics, complex macroeconomic reforms to ensure the transition
toward market economy without disruption, and the difficult implementation of adequate
and credible legal and regulatory frameworks. Therefore, the proposed project focuses on
limited implementable priority actions to improve the commercial orientation of the
beneficiaries, improve the contractual arrangements, increase competition and improve
operational efficiency of the sector.



- 13 -
2. THE POWER MARKET
A. AN OVERVIEW
2.1   The Tuoketuo Thermal Power Project is located in Inner Mongolia, and is being
developed to meet growing electricity demand in the north China power system, which
covers three provinces (Hebei, Shanxi, West Inner Mongolia) and two municipalities
(Beijing, Tianjin). Most of the project output will be exported to the Beijing area where
electricity demand has been suppressed but still increased at about 8 percent per year
through the first half of the 1990s. In 1995 the city's peak electricity demand was
4,091 MW and consumption was 22,260 GWh, corresponding to a load factor of 62.1
percent. Generation in Beijing in that year was 13,000 GWh, so that 41.6 percent of
electricity consumed was imported, primarily from the West Inner Mongolia and Shanxi.
2.2  Inner Mongolia has proven reserves of more than 200 billion tons of good-quality
coal. Transport limitation have restricted the development of this potential. Therefore,
the central government is promoting the development of mine-mouth power plants in
inland coal rich provinces (especially Shanxi Province and Inner Mongolia Autonomous
Region) and transfer of electricity to important load centers.
2.3   There are two separate grids in Inner Mongolia, the East Inner Mongolia Power
Grid that is connected to the Northeast Power Network and the West Inner Mongolia
Power Grid (WIMPG) that is part of the north China power system. The WIMPG is a net
exporter of electricity. It is connected to the Beijing-Tianjin-Tangshan power grid
(referred to as Jing-Jin-Tang power grid or JJTPG). The JJTPG is a major consuming
center owing to the concentration of high urban population and industries. Power
exchanges occur through two 500 kV lines (Datong to Fangshan) and (Fengzheng to
Shalingzi). There is also a 220 kV interconnection. The power and energy transmitted
from the WIMPG to the JJTPG are shown in Table 2.1.
TABLE 2.1: ELECTRICITY EXPORTS FROM THE WIMPG TO THE JJTPG
Annual
1990   1991   1992   1993   1994   1995   Growth (%)
Power (MW)       200      230    270    370    450    900        35
Energy (GWh)     616    1,193   1,713   2,225   3,086   4,286    47
2.4   Starting from 1990, along with the increase of generating capacity and power
output, the WIMPG began to transfer power to the JJTPG. The electricity exports
increased sixfold during 1990-95 from 0.6 to 4.2 TWh. This trend is expected to



-14 -
continue since numerous studies (especially the joint SPC/World Bank study on
Investment Strategies for China's Coal and Delivery System and the least-cost study
carried out during the preparation of the proposed project) demonstrated the economic
and environmental benefits to generate power in the northwest coal bases and transfer it
to important load centers in the northeast. Inner Mongolia has been listed as the major
electric power base to supply energy to the Jing-Jin-Tang area. The Inner Mongolia
Electric Power Company (IMEPC) has plans to increase its power exports up to 10 GW,
half of its installed capacity by the year 2000.
B. LOAD FORECAST
2.5   The load forecasts for the WIMPG and the JJTPG during the period 1996-2010
were prepared by IMEPC and NCPGC for the Ninth Five-Year Plan. The load
forecasting is based on a combination of econometric and analytical methods. The
econometric methods are based on the premise that electricity conservation efforts would
be sustained to keep the 0.8-0.9 elasticity of electricity consumption to GNP experienced
in the early 1990s. The analytical methods are based on (a) market surveys and
development plans of the major electricity consumers to determine the sectoral output
growths, and (b) electricity intensities for the different sectors to determine future
electricity consumptions taking into account load management and electricity
conservation measures. The corresponding load forecasts are summarized in Table 2.2.

2.6   During the next decade, the peak demand would increase by about 14,000 MW in
JJTPG and about 2,000 MW in the WIMPG. While the capacity addition requirements in
the WIMPG are relatively modest, the potential for electricity transfer to the JJTPG and
the Beijing region is considerable. The proposed project (1,200 MW) accounts for less
than 10 percent of the capacity needed to alleviate shortages and meet future electricity
requirements in the JJTPG. It is highly unlikely that it will encounter significant market
risks even in case of full scale development of the site (3,600 MW).



- 15 -
C. THE ROLE OF THE PROJECT
2.7   The proposed project addresses development of the North China power system in
an integrated approach based on (a) increase of electricity supply through creation of an
independent generation company (TEPGC) to develop a mine-mouth power plant in Inner
Mongolia and insure adequate supply to the Jing-Jin-Tang area through long-term
contractual arrangements; and (b) a transmission and distribution (T&D) component
including investments in the transmission system to improve the reliability and quality of
supply in Beijing and in rehabilitation of a pilot distribution area to reduce losses and
improve efficiency.
2.8   The proposed project, located in Tuoketuo county of the Inner Mongolia
Autonomous region would ease the power shortage in the JJTPG area, which includes the
fast-growing municipalities of Beijing and Tianjin.  It was selected following
consideration of different alternatives, mainly construction of plants close to the load
centers in the JJTPG or construction of mine-mouth plants in Shanxi Province and
transmission of the electricity to the JJTPG. Building the facilities in Tuoketuo was
found to be the most economically and environmentally attractive option.
2.9    Overall, the project is expected to have the least detrimental impacts on the
environment, compared to available alternatives. The plants to be constructed under the
project will introduce the first two 600 MW baseload units in West Inner Mongolia
system. The units are designed to meet strict atmospheric emission standards and
minimize other environmental impacts. The new capacity additions will result in reduced
use, and retirement of older, less efficient and more polluting plants.
2.10  Investment in the transmission and distribution networks in Beijing over the last
decade have not kept pace with the increase in consumer demand. Consequently, the
systems, especially in the city center, are characterized by: (a) losses that are above
economic levels, (b) a reliability level that is lower than that appropriate for the country's
capital city, and (c) inadequate voltage regulation in some areas. To ensure adequate
supplies, it is necessary to introduce more injection points at higher transmission voltages
closer to the city center and to upgrade the heavily loaded distribution system. These
measures will improve supply reliability, reduce losses and improve the quality of
consumer service. The component will achieve these objectives through construction of
new substations, installing reactive compensation, increasing transformer capacities in
existing substations, installing more efficient distribution transformers and increasing the
sizes of conductors on many distribution lines. These investments are needed to ensure
that the power to be transmitted from the Tuoketuo power station to Beijing can be
efficiently relayed to end-users with accepted standards of quality.



- 16-
3. THE BENEFICIARIES
3.1   There will be two beneficiaries of the proposed World Bank loan for the
implementation of two independent project components. The first beneficiary is the
Tuoketuo Electric Power Generation Company (TEPGC). TEPGC will be responsible
for the construction and operation of the first phase of the Tuoketuo coal-fired power
plant (2x600 MW) in the Inner Mongolia Autonomous Region. The second beneficiary
is NCPGC. NCPGC will be responsible for the rehabilitation and reinforcement of the
T&D system in Beijing.
A. LEGAL STATUS AND ORGANIZATION OF THE BENEFICIARIES
3.2   TEPGC. TEPGC is a newly formed limited liability company established in May
1996 by three shareholders, including NCPGC, the Inner Mongolia Electric Power
Company (IMEPC) and the Beijing International Electric Power Development
Investment Company (BIEPDIC). Its registered capital is Y 2 billion. NCPGC holds 51
percent of the shares, IMEPC 34 percent and BIEPDIC 15 percent.
3.3   The Articles of Association (dated May 3, 1996) empower TEPGC to build and
operate the Tuoketuo power plant to improve the power supply in the Jing-Jin-Tang area,
especially Beijing Municipality. They also require the company "to continuously
increase the economic profits and obtain higher returns for the shareholders." Under
existing Chinese laws, TEPGC can raise funds from various sources, issue stocks and
bonds and enter joint-venture agreements with foreign companies. However, these
activities are still regulated and government approval are required at various levels.
TEPGC has the authority to receive part of the proceeds of the proposed World Bank loan
through the Central Government, to carry out the construction and the management of the
power plant, and to enter into a Project Agreement with the World Bank.
3.4   TEPGC is headed by a General Manager assisted by one to four Deputy General
Managers and a Financial Officer. The organizational structure of TEPGC is shown in
Chart 2.  TEPGC is currently assuming full responsibility for preparation and
implementation of the project.  Initially, the Tuoketuo Power Plant Preparatory
Department had been established with the responsibility for preparation of the power
station component of the project. The staff in the Preparatory Department in IMEPC
have been transferred to TEPGC and continue to perform their previous functions. The
organization and proposed staffing of TEPGC are suitable for building and managing
large thermal power plants. A total staff complement of 588 staff is planned-459
operations staff and 128 managerial and administrative staff.  During the initial
construction phase, about 80 managerial and administrative staff would be required, of
which 50 would be retained when the plant is commissioned.



- 17-
3.5   The Articles of Association also provide for the establishment of a Board of
Directors to represent the interests of the shareholders, appoint and dismiss the general
manager and high-level staff and supervise the management of the Company. Members
of the Board are appointed by the shareholders.
3.6   NCPGC. NCPGC is one of the largest companies in China with an asset base of
about Y 23 billion. It has 47 wholly-owned subsidiary enterprises, 55 enterprises with a
controlling interest and 29 enterprises with a minority stake. NCPGC runs and operates
North China Power Grid. It supplies power to an area of 1.47 million square kilometers,
covering Beijing, Tianjin, Hebei, Shanxi and West Inner Mongolia. NCPGC owns 62
power stations, 4 survey and design institutes, 19 power construction companies, 5 test
and research organizations and 11 manufacturing and repairing enterprises.
3.7   NCPGC is an independent legal entity possessing the status of a limited liability
company. Its charter has been formulated under the law on industrial enterprises, and the
State Council's regulation on "the Structural Reformation of the North China Power
Network." NCPGC's legal status and Charter require revision to conform with the 1994
Company Law following issuance of guidelines for restructuring state-owned enterprises
as companies (Article 21, Chapter 2 of the Company Law).
3.8   NCPGC is headed by a President assisted by six Vice Presidents, a Chief
Engineer and a Chief Economist. The organizational structure is shown in Chart 3. The
functions of NCPGC are outlined in its existing charter. These include construction of
power projects and raising funds from various sources. In practice, NCPGC obtains
investment funds from state and central government and through borrowing from policy
and local banks. NCPGC has also corporate power to issue bonds and shares with the
approval of relevant authorities. It has the authority to receive part of the proceeds of the
proposed World Bank loan through the Central Government, to carry out the proposed
T&D component, and to enter into a Project Agreement with the World Bank.
3.9   Following the approval of the proposed project, NCPGC intends to transfer its 51
percent shareholding in TEPGC to the Beijing Datang Power Generation Company
Limited (BDP). BDP is a joint stock limited company incorporated in China with limited
liability (see Box 3.1 for more details). On March 21, 1997, BDP completed a "H" Share
Offer comprising: (a) a public offering of 2.5 percent of the registered capital in the
Hong Kong Stock Exchange and the London Stock Exchange; (b) a private placement of
22.5 percent of the registered capital to professional and institutional investors worldwide
by selective marketing in different markets.' The transfer of NCPGC's equity share in
TEPGC to BDP has been approved in principle by MOEP. Formal approval of the
relevant Chinese authorities is now being sought. Following approval by the Chinese
authorities, the transfer will be discussed formally with the Bank. It is expected that
Given the success of the offering, the Share Offer has been increased from 25 to 27.7 percent of the
registered capital.



- 18 -
TEPGC would continue to remain a fully independent limited liability company. BDP
plans to use a portion of the proceeds of the "H" Share Offering to finance its equity in
TEPGC.
Box 3.1: BEIJING DATANG POWER GENERATION COMPANY LIMITED
The Beijing Datang Power Generation Company (BDP) is a joint stock limited company
incorporated in China with limited liability. The Company was established in December, 1994 by NCPGC
to develop, construct, own and operate coal-fired electric power plants within the NCPGC service area.
BDP currently owns and operates 3,150 MW of power generation capacity, making it one of the
largest independent power producers in China. BDP plans to develop, either solely or with other investors,
several additional plants with an aggregate planned installed capacity of 4,500 MW by the year 2002.
BDP's effective interest in this additional planned capacity (i.e., the aggregate of the capacity of each plant
multipled by BDP's percentage interest in each plant) is anticipated to be 2,532 MW.
On March 21, 1997, BDP successfully completed a public and private offering of 25 percent of its
registered capital. This comprised 1,244,060,000 H Shares with a nominal value of Y 1.00 each. Each
share was priced at HK$2.52 per share, which was payable at full on application. Of the total share
offering, 10 percent was listed publicly on the Hong Kong Stock Exchange and London Stock Exchange,
and the remaining 90 percent placed with private institutional and professional investors worldwide by the
Underwriters to the issue. Given the success of the offering, the Share Offer has been increased from 25 to
27.7 percent of the registered capital.
