B. PLANNING, BUDGET AND CONTROL
3.10  Power bureaus and companies are responsible for developing annual and five-year
production and investment plans, which are integrated into the national plans and are
approved, through MOEP's auspices, by SPC. In the past, financial planning was
confined to attaching monetary values, predetermined by MOEP, to the power production
being planned. Although a power bureau's plan might be revised to reflect changes, it
would ordinarily not be revised to reflect differences between estimated prices and actual
prices. However, with the economic changes in the 1980s, prices have begun to move
toward market levels and the Government now finances investments through loans rather
than grants. Centralized control is being relaxed and replaced by greater financial
autonomy and responsibilities for the power entities. To address these changes, both
TEPGC and NCPGC will need to design and implement modem financial planning and
management systems.
3.11  MOEP recognizes the importance of financial management in the power sector in
China and is promoting relevant training programs for all the power institutions under its
control. Many of the power bureaus/companies, including NCPGC, have been equipped
with personal computers and software for planning, budgeting, and accounting functions.
Information from the bureaus/companies can be transferred to MOEP and consolidated
into regional and national data.
3.12  These systems represent a good first generation of automated management
information; however, they still are not sufficient and integrated as a network to promptly
provide financial and related statistical information about a power entity's operations to
the various management levels, supplying each level with the necessary details at the
appropriate times. TEPGC and NCPGC also need to introduce accounting and financial
management systems that will: (a) enable managers to be held accountable for costs of
activities and functions over which they have control; (b) provide information for
comparative analysis of actual costs with budgets for decision-making; and (c) provide
transparency of information for investors, government, lenders, and the public in general.
The proposed project includes a technical assistance component to support TEPGC and
NCPGC in establishing appropriate systems and procedures to upgrade their budgeting
and financial accounting systems, including provision of software and hardware, as well
as enhancement of their staff skills. The terms of reference (TOR) for the proposed
studies under this project are presented in Annex 3.1 for TEPGC and in Annex 3.2 for
NCPGC. Assurances were obtainedfrom TEPGC and NCPGC during negotiations that
they will: (a) carry out the organizational restructuring and financial management
systems studies according to the schedule agreed with the Bank; and (b) implement the
recommended organizational improvements, and accounting and financial management
systems, taking into account Bank comments.



-20 -
4. THE PROJECT
A. PROJECT OBJECTIVES
4.1   The proposed project is intended to:
(a)   increase electricity supply and electricity trade in North China through
creation of an independent power company to develop a mine-mouth
power plant in Inner Mongolia Autonomous Region that will supply the
Jing-Jin-Tang grid through long-term contractual arrangements;
(b)   improve the efficiency of energy supply and use in the region by:
(i) introducing modem technologies and implementing effective
operations and maintenance practices and procedures in power generation,
and (ii) reducing losses in the T&D systems of Beijing;
(c)   advance the power sector reform process in China by promoting the
development of electricity trade in North China through adequate and
market-oriented commercial arrangements;
(d)   diversify financing sources, improve the access of power entities to
international financial markets, and encourage private-sector investment in
existing and new power sector enterprises;
(e)   promote economic development and also improve soil conservation and
desertification control in Tuoketuo county.
4.2   Project impact would be monitored with respect to the above objectives. The key
indicators are:
PO (a) (i)    High availability of power units of Tuoketuo Thermal Power
Station
(ii)    Quantity of energy transferred from TEPGC to the North China
Power Grid
PO (b) (i)   Increased efficiency and reliability of T&D in the Greater Beijing
Area
PO (c) (i)   Implementation and adherence to commercial terms in the power
purchase contract between TEPGC and NCPGC



- 21 -
(ii)    Development of framework for the creation of a wholesale power
market in North China
PO (d) (i)    Divestiture of one power plant
(ii)    Additional equity offering in a listed power company
(iii)   Participation of external investors in the expansion of an existing
power company
PO (e) (i)    Acceleration of economic growth
(ii)   Increase in afforestation rate in Tuoketuo county.
4.3   The achievement of the objectives would be tracked and monitored through
specific measurable targets.  Project implementation in terms of construction and
commissioning would be monitored as actual physical output or percentage of project
completion (power generated, number of trees planted, etc.), achievement of efficiency
and quality of supply goals (availability, supply interruptions, system losses), and
improvement of management and advances in power sector reform (financial targets,
diversification of financing sources). These are presented in Section K (Reporting and
Monitoring).
B. PROJECT DESCRIPTION
4.4   The project consists of two major components. The first and largest component is
the Tuoketuo thermal power plant and associated technical assistance. The second is the
Beijing T&D component. The respective beneficiaries are TEPGC and NCPGC.
Tuoketuo Thermal Power Plant Component
4.5   The first component, under the responsibility of TEPGC, will include:
(a)   construction of the first two 600 MW coal-fired subcritical thermal units in
Inner Mongolia Autonomous Region as the first phase of the Tuoketuo
power station. These units will be equipped with on-line performance
monitoring systems to minimize coal consumption and gradually introduce
advanced maintenance techniques;
(b)   technical assistance to TEPGC for: (i) design and introduction of modern
accounting and financial management systems; (ii) construction
management and engineering services; (iii) environmental management
and monitoring; (iv) use of simulator and computerized systems to
develop operational staff capabilities in plant operation and management;
and (v) implementation of a plan for assets divestiture and involvement of
private investors in existing and new power projects in Inner Mongolia
(see para. 4.15); and



- 22 -
(c)   a desertification control and dryland management program to improve the
environmental conditions in Tuoketuo county.
4.6   The two generation units to be constructed under the proposed Tuoketuo thermal
power plant component consist of subcritical, drum-type boilers with low nitrogen oxide
(NOJ) burners, single-shaft condensing turbines and 600 MW generators. They share a
single 240 m stack. The plant's design coal is a blend of screened unwashed bituminous
coal with a calorific value of 4,300+500 kcal/kg, ash content of 26+4 percent and sulfur
content of 0.47 percent.  Emissions of particulates are minimized by electrostatic
precipitators with an efficiency of 99.76. The plant will also include an on-line
monitoring system to maximize efficiency and reduce coal consumption and provide
useful data for preventive maintenance (see Annex 4.1).
4.7   To transmit the electricity generated by the Tuoketuo thermal power plant to the
JJTPG grid, three 500 kV transmission lines and associated sub- and switching stations
will be constructed under a separate project that does not involve World Bank funding
(see Annex 4.1). Assurances were obtained during negotiations that NCPGC will
complete the 500 kV transmission lines and associated sub- and switching stations three
months prior to the commissioning of the first 600 MW unit of the Tuoketuo Thermal
Power Plant.
Beijing T&D Component
4.8   This second component, under the responsibility of NCPGC, will include:
(a)   construction of two 220 kV  indoor substations at Xibianmen and
Xizhimen respectively. Xibianmen will be equipped with two 250 MVA,
220/110/10 kV transformers and Xizhimen with two 200 MVA, 220/110/
10 kV transformers;
(b)   addition of a third 250 MVA  220/110/10 kV  transformer in the
underground Wangfujing substation, currently under construction; and
(c)   technical assistance to NCPGC for implementing accounting and financial
management systems.
In connection with, and to complement, this project, NCPGC will undertake the
following program, approved separately and financed locally.
(a)   Replacement of overloaded and inefficient units in the Beicheng and
Huichengmen substations by four 50 MVA 110/10 kV transformers; and
(b)   upgrading of the distribution system in the heavily loaded, high-growth
Xizhimen area. The upgrading will involve reconductoring of insulated
overhead lines (approximately 75 km), and installation of five compact
10 kV transformer stations.
A detailed description is shown in Annex 4.2.



- 23 -
Project Design and Consulting Services
4.9   The Tuoketuo thermal power project feasibility study and design report were
completed by the North China Electric Power Design Institute (NCEPDI) in 1993, and
revised in June 1996. The project was approved by SPC in 1994. Following the Bank's
Guidelines, international engineering consultants were selected to assist in finalizing
designs, procurement of goods, engineering and design interface coordination and limited
construction supervision. Funding in the amount of $2.4 million out of the China
Reform, Institutional Support and Preinvestment Project [CRISPP (Credit 2447-CHA)]
has been provided for this purpose. The scope of the construction management and
supervision component will be broadened under the proposed project according to the
TOR provided in Annex 4.3. A first version of the Environmental Impact Assessment
Report (EIAR) was prepared in 1994 by NCEPDI. The report was revised with the
assistance of international consultants to conform with the Chinese and Bank
environmental procedures and guidelines for thermal power projects and finalized in May
1996. The EIAR has been approved by all Government authorities, and its summary was
distributed to the Executive Directors on May 28, 1996. The overall project concept and
design is satisfactory.
4.10 For the Beijing T&D component, designs, including load flows, fault level, and
stability studies, have been carried out by NCEPDI. NCEPDI has extensive experience in
the successful design of T&D systems as well as of other power infrastructure facilities.
The concept and design of the transmission facilities and the pilot project of distribution
rehabilitation to reduce losses is satisfactory.  Assistance is being provided by
international consultants through a Beijing T&D efficiency improvement study funded
under a Policy and Human Resources Development (PHRD) grant (see para. 4.32).
C. SECTOR REFORMS
4.11  One of the objectives of the proposed project is to assist and facilitate the power
sector reform process in the North China region. It will advance the power sector reform
process in two main areas: (a) development of contractual arrangements between TEPGC
(the Seller) and NCPGC (the Purchaser) that will be based on efficient commercial and
economic terms; and (b) assistance for divestiture of existing assets and/or involvement
of domestic or foreign private investors to raise additional capital for power system
expansion.
Contractual Arrangements
4.12  The project will provide technical assistance in establishing TEPGC, the first
independent power producer on the NCPGC system, as a financially autonomous and
commercially oriented enterprise. The assistance includes development of a detailed
power purchase agreement and dispatch rules that will govern the business relationships
between TEPGC (the seller) and NCPGC (the purchaser). Once the concept of an
independent generating enterprise has been successfully demonstrated, privately owned
facilities will be more easily accepted. The power purchase agreement developed under



- 24 -
the proposed project and signed on December 31, 1996 is a major step toward full
commercialization and business orientation of the power sector in China.  Future
agreements with other nonutility and privately owned producers as well will build on the
progress achieved.
4.13  The PPA reflects the principles agreed with the World Bank as a part of the
continuing dialogue on power sector reform in China. The most innovative legal features
of the PPA for a Chinese power project are: (a) risks are allocated based on widely
accepted financing and commercial practices; and (b) explicit definition of government
change-of-law and policy events with provision of legal recourse and protection for both
the seller and the purchaser. From an economic efficiency perspective, a two-part tariff
has been defined for the first time in a Chinese power sales contract with: (a) a capacity
charge covering debt and a minimum equity return if the plant meets minimum contracted
availability; (b) a capacity charge weighted to provide higher payments for peak
availability; (c) an energy charge reflecting only fuel and other variable costs-there is no
minimum energy generation (or take-or-pay) obligation, ensuring economic dispatch of
the facility; and (d) incentives built into the capacity charge to reduce overall scheduled
and unscheduled outage time for higher returns.
4.14 The project will also demonstrate the viability of the purchasing agency model,
which is becoming the standard approach to unbundling generation and transmission in
many other countries. The regional grid will purchase electricity from various generators
and sell to the distribution entities at prices that reflect the overall cost of supply. In
subsequent stages, the functions of the regional grid company would eventually be
limited to purchase of power from independent producers and other power generation
companies, ownership and operation of the transmission system (including dispatch), and
sale of power to the provincial utilities.
Assets Divestiture
4.15  During project processing, IMEPC prepared, with the assistance of international
consultants funded by Canadian Trust Funds, a plan aimed at increasing private and
nonutility investment in the Inner Mongolia Generation System. IMEPC's divestiture
plan includes: (a) additional equity offering of 15 million shares of Inner Mongolia
Mendian Huaneng Thermal Power Company in Shanghai (about Y 4/share is expected by
the underwriter for shares with a face value of Y 1/share) by mid-1997; (b) establishing a
limited liability company to support the sale of the Wulashan coal-fired power plant
(200 MW) to an independent Chinese operator by the end of 1997; and (c) involving
private investor(s) in further expansion and development of the Haibowan Electric Power
Company (2xlO0 MW  installed and 2x200 MW  under development).  Technical
assistance will be provided under the proposed project to carry out preparatory work to
implement IMEPC's plans for divestiture and involvement of private investors in existing
and new projects (see Annex 4.4).



- 25 -
D. ENVIRONMENTAL CONSIDERATIONS
4.16  In accordance with OD 4.01 (Environmental Assessment), the project has been
assigned to Category A. The Environmental Assessment (EA) was prepared with
assistance of an international consultant specializing in environmental issues related to
thermal power plants, and has been approved by the Inner Mongolia Environmental
Protection Agency (IMEPA), and the National Environmental Protection Agency
(NEPA). The EA has been reviewed by the World Bank; it is considered that all
environmental aspects of the project are satisfactorily addressed with respect to all
Chinese and World Bank environmental regulations, policies, and procedures. The
project has been designed and will be implemented in accordance with modern concepts
of environmental management. Consequently, the project should cause minimum
disturbance to the environment. An environmental management unit (EMU) will be
established by the company (see Annex 4.5 for EMU TOR) and a consultant engaged to
assist in defining and operationalizing the EMU work program (see AnnexA4.6) for
Consultant TOR). An environmental monitoring program will be incorporated to
ascertain and verify these conditions on a sustained basis.
4.17  The project is a greenfield operation, located within the boundary of Yanshanying
a small village in a rural area of Tuoketuo County. The site is approximately 70 km
southwest of Huhhot City, the provincial capital of Inner Mongolia Autonomous Region.
Three alternative locations for the power plant were considered.  All three were
satisfactory from an environmental perspective. The selected site was closest to the ash
disposal site with the most favorable geological conditions, was most convenient to
infrastructure (drainage, water supply, transportation facilities, etc.) and offered the best
natural geologic foundation for plant construction.
4.18  Modern pollution control technologies will be incorporated into the design of the
power plant, such as high-efficiency electrostatic precipitators (ESPs) and low NO,
burners. In addition, the plant layout provides adequate space to install additional
pollution control systems (e.g., flue gas desulfurization) if required, as a result of future
changes in Chinese emission standards and/or technological developments permit.
Annex 4.7. Part A provides a summary of key environmental issues associated with the
project, their anticipated potential impacts, the mitigation plan to assure these potential
impacts are minimized, the monitoring program with which actual environmental impacts
will be measured, compared with EA predictions and, if necessary, indicate further
actions to be taken. As mentioned above, further strengthening of TEPGC with respect to
environmental management capabilities will be included in the project, with the creation
of the EMU, acquisition of appropriate environmental monitoring equipment, and the
establishing of an automated environmental data management system (see Annex 4.7e
Parts B. C. and D). In addition, since it is anticipated that the power plant will act as a
nucleus for further economic development in the region, technical assistance (training,
both locally and abroad) will be provided through TEPGC to the environmental
protection bureaus (EPBs) of Inner Mongolia, Huhhot, and Tuoketuo so that they can



- 26 -
prepare an air quality management plan to ensure healthy air quality levels are preserved
in the region.
4.19  Environmental issues associated with the project (power station, transmission line,
railway connection and cooling water supply system) and addressed in the EA included
all concerns expressed by affected peoples in a series of public meetings. The local
population and the local authorities support the project and the mitigating measures
recommended in the EA. The technical assistance programs, mitigation and monitoring
programs presented in Annex 4.7 will be fully implemented.
4.20  Key issues addressed in the EA included: air pollution, (dust, SO2), noise, water
pollution (groundwater at the ash disposal site, and surface water contamination of the
Yellow River), electric field strength, bird flight patterns (transmission lines), industrial
hazards and worker safety, desertification control, and impacts of both construction and
operation labor forces on the local infrastructure and social structure. The distribution
system component is mostly rehabilitation and reinforcement of existing lines and
substations, and will not adversely impact the environment. Public safety will be
improved. No polychlorinated biphenyls (PCBs) will be utilized in the substations to be
financed, and bidding documents will note this restriction accordingly. MOEP provided
certification that the transformers that are to be replaced under the T&D component do
not contain PCBs (banned in China from the early 1970s). In addition, the proposed
project (two units of 600 MW each) is the first phase of an overall program to develop
3,600 MW at the Tuoketuo site. To anticipate any future adverse environmental
consequences, the EA examined both the immediate impacts of the proposed project
(1,200 MW), as well as cumulative impacts of the 3,600 MW program.
4.21  All mitigating measures (for air, water, solids, noise, electric field, etc.)
incorporated in the project are designed to meet appropriate Chinese requirements and/or
World Bank guidelines, whichever is stricter. In the absence of either, international
standards or codes of practice will be used. Assurances were obtained at negotiations
that TEPGC will carry out the Environment Action Plan in a manner satisfactory to the
Bank. In addition, a soil conservation and desertification control subcomponent of the
project would contribute to the revegetation effort currently planned (see Annex 4.8).
The plan calls for the arrest of desertification through soil conservation techniques,
establishment of vegetative cover to combat wind erosion, and improvement of soil
microclimate, water retention and nutrient properties. The overall target for Tuoketuo
County is to establish a 40 percent forest cover. Local people will be employed in the
revegetation effort, with preference given to oustees.
E. RESETTLEMENT
4.22  The project design focused on minimizing the scope of resettlement. Where land
acquisition and demolition of existing housing are unavoidable, the Resettlement Action
Plans (RAPs) are proposed to ensure: (a) improvement of the standard of living of the
Project-Affected Persons (PAPs); (b) compensation of the villages for land and PAPs for
their lost assets at replacement values without depreciation; (d) compensation for the



- 27 -
disrupted infrastructure; and (e) establishment of adequate mechanisms for grievance and
appeals, and systems for internal independent monitoring. Both project components
involve land acquisition and/or relocation of population. Costs of land acquisition and
resettlement are estimated at Y 296.5 million for both components (see Annex4.92).
Resettlement Action Plans (RAPs) have been prepared to comply with appropriate
Chinese policies and/or World Bank guidelines (OD 4.30). Assurances were obtained
that the Borrower would ensure that the persons affected by the project are resettled in
accordance with the resettlement plans agreed with the Beneficiaries.
Tuoketuo Thermal Power Plant
4.23  The plant requires: (a) acquisition of about 836 ha of land, of which 55.6 percent
is cultivated, and (b) demolition of about of 3,340 m  of old houses with poor access to
basic infrastructure and sanitation facilities. It will affect about 2,253 persons. RAPs
satisfactory to the Bank have been prepared for all subcomponents for which detailed
designs are already complete (power plant and ash disposal site). Future resettlement
activities related to the railway connection and the cooling water supply system (water
pumping station and pipeline) will be undertaken more than two years later. The designs
for these two subcomponents are being carried out by the Zhungeer Coal Industry
Company and the Yellow River Hydraulic and Water Resource Administration
Committee. The components are linked to broader development of the Zhungeer Coal
Mine-Fengzhen railway project and the management of the Yellow River. The RAPs for
these components will be prepared by mid-1997 following the completion of the design
of the railway connection and the cooling water supply system.
4.24  Arrangements for relocation, prepared in collaboration with local authorities and
the population affected by the project, are in line with Chinese national and provincial
policies. Special attention was given to the most vulnerable groups (households headed
by women, senior people and poorest households). The new village is within 300 meters
of the existing village, and 250 meters of the Huhhot-Zhungeer highway. All new houses
will be built of bricks and wood and are of higher quality than the old ones. New public
facilities will be provided in the new village such as a clinic, school, shopping and
cultural centers. Displaced farmers will continue their agricultural activities in the newly
reclaimed agricultural lands in new irrigation areas. Four motor-pumped wells will be
installed for irrigation purposes. Assurances were obtained at negotiations that TEPGC
will (a) carry out the resettlement program for the Tuoketuo Thermal Power Plant and
ash disposal site in accordance with the agreed resettlement plan; and (b) prepare a RAP
for the railway connection and the cooling water supply system by September 30, 1997
and carry out the resettlement program as agreed with the Bank.
4.25  A Project Resettlement Office with six staff members has been established in
TEPGC to oversee the management and implementation of resettlement program. It will
be assisted by Tuoketuo County Resettlement Office and Yanshanying Township
Resettlement Office. Two senior members of the office have received training on
involuntary resettlement sponsored by MOF.



- 28 -
Beijing T&D
4.26 Xibianmen substation requires acquisition of 6,100 mn of land, demolition of 239
houses, and relocation of 935 persons. However, this program is part of a wider planned
program of urban renovation and development of Beijing under the responsibility of the
Beijing Municipal Construction Development Corporation (BMCDC). The majority of
residential houses to be demolished are old and hazardous with poor sanitation facilities.
There is no resettlement need for Wangfujing and Xizhimen because the former is an
underground substation under construction and the latter will be built using the site of the
existing 110 kV substation. The pilot project of distribution rehabilitation involves
mainly reconductoring and installation of compact transformers and capacitors and does
not require any resettlement. Households displaced by the Xibianmen substation will be
relocated in new apartment buildings with modem facilities in a close-by residential
areas. Assurances were obtained that NCPGC will carry out resettlement under the
Beijing T&D component according to the agreed resettlement plan.
4.27  A Project Resettlement Office has been established to oversee the resettlement
work related to Xibianmen substation. It consists of representatives from Beijing
Municipal Power Supply Company (BMPSC), which is a division of NCPGC, BMCDC,
the Government of Xicheng District, and Beijing Municipality.
Implementation Monitoring
4.28 TEPGC and NCPGC respectively contracted the Inner Mongolia Academy of
Social Science (IMASS) and the Beijing Social and Psychological Research Institute
(BSPRI) to monitor the resettlement activities and provide an independent evaluation of
resettlement. The monitoring and evaluation will be based on a sample of 10 to 15
percent of affected population. The objective of monitoring and evaluation is to ensure
that the resettlement program is implemented smoothly and that living conditions and
income of resettlers are fully restored.
F. PROJECT IMPLEMENTATION
Implementation Schedule
4.29 TEPGC will be responsible for implementation and operation of the Tuoketuo
thermal power plant component. Its organizational structure is shown in Chart 2.
Preparatory works including access roads and construction power supply started in
October 1996 and have been progressing satisfactorily. The bidding documents for the
procurement of the main boiler and turbine-generator packages were issued in April 1997
and are scheduled to be opened in September 1997. The commercial operation of the first
generating unit is expected in October 2001 and the second unit in September 2002.
Thus, the project completion date would be January 2004 and the closing date of the
Bank loan would be July 31, 2004. Chart 4 presents the implementation schedule for the
power plant. Furthermore, key dates of project implementation are given in Annex 4.10.
4.30 NCPGC will be responsible for execution of the Beijing T&D component
including construction, commissioning and operation. NCPGC has had substantial



- 29 -
experience in power generation, T&D, and has been operating the North China Power
Grid since 1987. Beijing Power Supply Design Institute (BPSDI) and NCEPDI will
undertake preparation of technical bidding documents, bids evaluation, detailed
construction design and supply of drawings for, respectively, Xizhimen and Wangfujing
and Xibianmen.  BPSDI and NCEPDI have both design experience in 220 kV
substations. Construction of the works will be the responsibility of the construction
department of BMPSC. The construction department also has extensive experience in the
relevant area. The bidding documents for the items to be procured under ICB packages
are scheduled to be issued in June 1997. Completion of the T&D component is planned
for August 1999. Key dates for implementation are given in Annex 4.10 and the
component implementation schedule is given in Chart 5.
Management Development and Training
4.31  To meet TEPGC's long-term staffing requirements and to further develop
TEPGC's management capability, a comprehensive technical assistance and training
program  is included under the proposed project.  It consists of the following:
(a) technical assistance to implement accounting and financial management systems (see
para. 3.12 and Annex 3.1); (b) training in utility management and financial planning;
(c) project-related training for technical staff; and (d) upgrading of and equipment for
TEPGC's training facilities. It is planned that all the technical staff will be trained in
various fields, totaling 1,325 staff-months of training in China and abroad (see Annex
4.11). Assurances were obtained from TEPGC that it will carry out the management
development and training program as agreed with the Bank.
4.32 NCPGC's planning and management capability will be strengthened through
training and studies. A technical assistance program is included to assist NCPGC to
implement accounting and financial management systems (see para. 3.12 and Annex 3.2).
A Beijing T&D efficiency improvement study is being carried out to develop a program
of investments and other measures that would reduce power losses on the Beijing T&D
systems to economic levels and ensure acceptable quality and reliability of power
supplies to end-consumers. The study is funded through a PHRD grant for an amount of
$960,000. The study will strengthen NCPGC's planning capability through technology
transfer and on-the-job training in advanced planning methodology and provision of
hardware and software for transmission and distribution system analyses.

H. FINANCING PLAN
4.34 The total financing requirements (including IDC of $187.2 million equivalent) are
estimated at $1,290.8 million equivalent. The financing plan for the overall project is
shown in Table 4.2. The financing required for the Tuoketuo thermal power plant
component is estimated at $1,108.4 million equivalent, including IDC of $153.6 million
equivalent. The local costs estimated at $524.8 million equivalent, IDC for both local
and foreign loans estimated at $153.6 million equivalent and working capital estimated at
$6.0 million equivalent will be provided by equity contribution and local borrowings
raised by the three shareholders. Their equity contribution is estimated at $277.2 million
equivalent, representing 25 percent of the total cost of the component. The remaining
financing requirements of about $401.2 million equivalent will be covered through bond
issues and borrowings by the three shareholders proportionally to their respective shares
in TEPGC, specifically, 51 percent by NCPGC, 34 percent by IMEPC, and 15 percent by
BIPDIC.
4.35  The foreign cost of the thermal power plant component, excluding IDC of $64.1
million, estimated at $430 million will be covered by the proposed Bank loan of $330
million and commercial cofinancing of about $100.0 million. The borrower of the
commercial cofinancing would be the People's Republic of China represented by MOF,
and the proceeds of the financing would be onlent to TEPGC under the same
arrangements as the Bank loan. It is expected that the central government, represented by
the Ministry of Finance, will seek amortized syndication loans with 15-year maturities
from commercial banks/insurance companies with a LIBOR-based floating interest rate to
better match the project's cash flow.  The Government gave assurances that the
commercial cofinancing will be in place in December 1998. It is expected that provision
of an optional cross-default clause by the Bank would be sufficient to secure adequate
loan terms. However, if required by market conditions, the Bank would consider
provision of a credit guarantee to extend maturity and lower the cost of commercial
borrowings by China. The Bank would then require a Government counterindemnity
against covered risks and would charge a guarantee fee of 0.25 percent per year of the
Bank exposure under the guarantee. Such a guarantee operation, if required, would be
submitted to the Board for approval on a stand-alone basis.
4.36 The financing required for the Beijing T&D component is estimated at $182.4
million equivalent, including IDC of $33.6 million equivalent. The proposed Bank loan
of $70 million will finance the foreign cost of the investment. The local costs estimated
at $78.8 million equivalent and the IDC for both foreign and local loans of $33.6 million
equivalent will be covered by self-financing from NCPGC and loans from SDB, Beijing
Municipality, and Hebei Province.

4.37 The proposed Bank loan of $400 million will be made to the People's Republic of
China at the Bank's standard interest rate for LIBOR-based US dollar single currency
loans, with a maturity of 20 years, including a 5-year grace period. Proceeds of the loan
would be onlent from the Government to TEPGC and NCPGC on the same terms and
conditions as the Bank loan. TEDGC and NCPGC will bear the foreign exchange risk.
TEPGC and NCPGC have selected LIBOR-based US dollar single currency loan terms
for the project to: (a) facilitate management of the foreign exchange risk of their
borrowing by more closely matching the currency of their liabilities with that of their net
trade flows, about 75 percent of which are US dollar denominated; and (b) preserve the
full maturity of the loan and the lower annuity payment in early years. The fixed-rate
option would have resulted in a 15-year loan and higher annual payments during the
initial operational period.
4.38  Assurances were obtainedfrom the Government that it would onlend the proceeds
of the proposed Bank loan to TEPGC and NCPGC under subsidiary loan agreements



- 33 -
with a term of 20 years, including 5-year grace period, at a variable rate equal to the
Bank rate. TEPGC and NCPGC will bear the foreign exchange risk. Execution of the
subsidiary loan agreements between the Government and both TEPGC and NCPGC,
satisfactory to the Bank and approval of the Loan Agreement by the State Council would
be conditions of loan effectiveness.
I. PROCUREMENT
4.39 Procurement arrangements are summarized in Table 4.3 and the related
procurement schedules for various packages are given in Annex 4.12. The Bank's
Procurement Guidelines (published in January 1995 and revised in January and August
1996) will be applied to all Bank-financed procurement. The Bank loan will be largely
disbursed against major plant equipment, boiler and turbine-generator packages and part
of the T&D equipment and materials through international competitive bidding (ICB).
Other procurement packages, such as instrumentation and control, 500 kV electrical
equipment, ash-handling equipment, etc., will be funded by commercial bank/insurance
company loans. For procurement of goods not financed by the Bank, the Borrower may
adopt other procurement procedures. In such cases, the Bank shall be satisfied that the
procedures fulfill the Borrower's obligations to cause the project to be carried out
diligently and efficiently. Prequalification of bidders is not required because the
equipment to be procured under the project is "quasi-standardized" (i.e., the technical
specifications are clearly specified and all potential suppliers are familiar with the
Chinese market). Postqualification of bidders will be based on criteria set out in the
bidding documents. Some specialized equipment estimated to cost less than $300,000
per contract up to an aggregate amount of $6 million for the thermal power plant
component and $1.2 million for the Beijing T&D component and which can only be
purchased from a limited number of suppliers, regardless of the cost thereof, will be
procured through limited international bidding (LIB).  Goods, instruments and
accessories needed for construction and operation of the power plant, including
equipment for environmental monitoring and training, which are readily available off-the-
shelf or are standard specification commodities, and which are estimated to cost less than
$300,000 per contract up to an aggregate amount of $2.5 million for the thermal power
plant component and $600,000 for the Beijing T&D component, will be procured through
contracts awarded on the basis of (a) international shopping procedures for up to an
aggregate amount not to exceed $2 million for the thermal power component and
$400,000 for the T&D component, or (b) national shopping procedures for up to an
aggregate amount not to exceed $500,000 for the thermal power plant component and
$200,000 for the Beijing T&D component. Goods of a proprietary nature may be
procured through direct contracting with the Bank's prior agreement up to an aggregate
amount not to exceed $500,000 for the thermal power plant component and $200,000 for
the Beijing T&D component.

4.40  Five contracts amounting to about $7.3 million in total for consulting services for
the two components will be awarded in accordance with Bank Guidelines for Use of
Consultants (August 1981). These include: engineering consultant for construction
management (one contract); organizational restructuring and financial management
systems (two contracts); consulting services for environmental aspects (one contract); and
legal and financial services for asset divestiture. For complex, time-based assignments,
such contracts shall be based on the standard form of contract for consultants' services
issued by the Bank, with such modifications thereto as shall have been agreed by the
Bank.
4.41  In ICB procurement, model bidding documents developed in consultation with the
Bank would be used. Standard bid evaluation forms for procurement of Goods and
Works (April 1996) would be used. Qualified domestic manufacturers would be eligible
for a margin of preference of 15 percent of the CIF price or the actual customs duty,
whichever is lower, in the evaluation of bids.
4.42  Because of foreign exchange limitations, civil works (fully locally financed) and
non-Bank-financed equipment will be procured locally through competitive bidding
procedures acceptable to the Bank. The procurement procedures for non-Bank-financed
components will not adversely affect the execution of the project in terms of cost, quality
or completion time.
4.43  Prior review would apply to all Bank-financed contracts for goods, with an
estimated cost of $5 million equivalent or above (about 94 percent of the loan). Other
contracts would be subject to selective postaward review. Prior review would also be
required for all consultancy contracts with an estimated cost exceeding $50,000 for
individuals, or $100,000 for firms. Exceptions to prior review would not apply to the
TOR for all consultancy contracts and single-source selection of consulting firms.
4.44  The invitation to prequalify or bid for each contract estimated to cost $10,000,000
equivalent or more shall be advertised in accordance with the procedures applicable to
large contracts under para. 2.8 of the Guidelines.
J. DISBURSEMENT
4.45  The Bank loan will be disbursed against:  (a) 100 percent of the foreign
expenditures for directly imported equipment and materials quoted on a CIF basis;
(b) 100 percent of local expenditures ex-factory for locally manufactured items, and 75
percent of local expenditures for other items procured locally; and (c) 100 percent of the
expenditure for consulting services and training. For expenditures pertaining to goods
contracts valued at less than $5,000,000 (except for the first three such contracts),
consultancy contracts valued at less than $100,000 for firms and $50,000 for individuals
and all training, reimbursement will be made on the basis of Statements of Expenditures.
Supporting documents need not be submitted to the Bank but will be retained in
TEPGC's and NCPGC's office for review by Bank supervision missions. To facilitate
disbursements under this project, a Special Account will be established for each



- 36 -
Beneficiary with an authorized allocation of $15 million for TEPGC and $8 million for
NCPGC, representing approximately four months of average project disbursements.
Applications for replenishment will be submitted monthly or when the amounts
withdrawn equal 50 percent of the initial deposit, whichever comes sooner. Annex 4.13
presents the disbursement schedule for the proposed  Bank  loan  as well as a standard
profile of disbursements for power sector in China. The disbursements are expected to be
completed in six years for the power plant and three years for the T&D component, very
close to the standard disbursement profile for the power sector in China. The last year is
for payment of retention money. The annual estimated contractual and other payments

K. REPORTING AND MONITORING
4.46  Understandings were reached at appraisal concerning procedures and satisfactory
performance indicators for monitoring and reporting.  Reporting would include
semiannual project implementation reports covering all project components. A rolling
financial plan would also be submitted annually. The scope and content of the project
progress reports have also been agreed. The Bank supervision plan attached in Annex
4.14 will focus on the core activities of project implementation and achievement of
agreed objectives. The Key Project Performance Indicators are described in Annex 4.15.
4.47  With regard to the construction of the Tuoketuo power plant, monitoring of costs,
quality and schedule is the main function of TEPGC and their consultants during the
coming years. The Bank will monitor procurement progress in accordance with the
procurement schedule in Annex 4.12, and construction progress in accordance with the
schedule of Charts 4 and 5 and key dates presented in Annex 4. 10. Overall costs will be
monitored in accordance with Table 4.1. The guiding principles and implementation
indicators for the resettlement are or will be described in the RAPs submitted to the Bank
or covenanted under the proposed project.  The responsibility for environmental
monitoring is comprehensively presented in Annex 4.7.

5. FINANCIAL ASPECTS
A. BACKGROUND
5.1   TEPGC is a newly created corporation. In the medium term, its activities will be
dominated by the construction and subsequent operation of the proposed project.
TEPGC's operation and finances are interrelated with those of NCPGC; sole purchaser of
all power generated by the proposed two units. Both TEPGC and NCPGC follow MOF
financial regulations applicable to state-owned enterprises.
5.2   The financial regulations promulgated by MOF have gradually evolved over the
last decade from a system in which all investment funds were provided by the
Government and all surpluses remitted to it, to one which provides some autonomy and
incentives for efficiency. Overall, the new accounting principles effective July 1, 1993
are generally consistent with international accounting standards and financial rules. They
will, together with the rationalization of state assets management, and other fiscal and
financial sector reforms, further increase the accountability of the management of power
entities.
5.3   The Government promulgated a series of important tax laws at the end of 1993.
An unified corporate income tax rate of 33 percent is being applied to all domestic
enterprises. A new 17 percent value-added tax (VAT) replaced the old 25 percent sales
tax for power generation and distribution. The new tax policy is more transparent and
eliminates the lengthy and complicated negotiations between enterprises and the
Government. It is also conducive to better planning and financial management in the
power companies.
5.4   One of the Government's sectoral objectives is to strengthen the enterprises'
financial management so that the power companies could perform their increased
financial role effectively. Toward this end, the proposed project would focus on the
institutional building aspect and include (a) technical assistance to develop and improve
the accounting and financial management systems of both beneficiaries (para. 3.11); and
(b) agreements with TEPGC and NCPGC on financial performance targets to ensure
disciplined and sound financial management (paras. 5.7 and 5.15).
B. TEPGC's FINANCES
5.5   As TEPGC is a new company and has very few activities, the past financial
performance of the three sponsors, including NCPGC, IMEPC, and BIEPDIC, were
reviewed and found satisfactory. The review was focused on the cash flow situation of
each entity in order to assess the availability of funds to finance the proposed project.



- 39 -
Financial Performance Targets
5.6   The self-financing ratio used in other power projects in China is not adequate to
monitor TEPGC's financial performance. TEPGC is an independent (nonutility) power
producer, and unlike power utilities, its activities do not require regular investments.
Moreover, development of subsequent phases may take time and could involve other
domestic and/or foreign investors. Therefore, a rate of return on equity has been selected
because it is a performance indicator commonly recognized in financial markets and
widely used by the power industry. Moreover, it is consistent with the tariff fornula set
in the power purchase agreement signed by TEPGC and NCPGC.
5.7   With a view to promoting prudent financial management, assurances were
obtained from TEPGC that it would:
(a)   take all necessary measures, including but not limited to tariff
adjustments, to earn an annual return on equity (defined as the total
unimpaired paid-in captial) of not less than 20 percent in 2003 and
thereafter;
(b)   take all necessary measures, including but not limited to tariff
adjustments, to ensure its internal cash generation would provide a debt
service coverage ratio of no less than 1.3 times during 2001-03, and 1.5
times thereafter; and
(c)   by April 30 of each year, furnish to the Bank a rolling eight-yearfinancial
plan containing projected income statements, sources and uses of funds,
and balance sheets.
Future Finances
5.8   As noted above (para. 4.12), a power purchase agreement has been signed
between TEPGC and NCPGC, which provides for a rate of return of 15 percent on equity
investment after the distribution of net income for 30 percent of principal repayment and
allocation of 15 percent for two funds during the first 10 years' operation and 20 percent
during the remaining years to ensure TEPGC's financial viability. Based on the
assumptions detailed in Annex 5.2, including electricity tariff setting that would allow
TEPGC to achieve the above financial performance targets, projections of TEPGC's
finances for the period 2001-2008 are presented in Annex 5.1. Salient features of
TEPGC's future finances are highlighted in Table 5.1.
5.9   Revenue Position. The first unit is scheduled to be commissioned in 2001 and
the second unit in 2002. The two units would become fully operational on a commercial
basis in 2003. The financial forecast indicated that the average tariff would be about 35.8
fen/kWh in 2001 (24.05 fen in end-1995 prices), which confirms the competitiveness of
the Tuoketuo Power Plant. The consequent cash generation is projected to be sufficient
to cover the debt service requirements. During the first eight years of its operations, the



- 40 -
average tariff that would allow TEPGC to achieve the agreed financial performance
targets and meet the required net income distribution is estimated at 47.8 fen/kWh in
current terms and 25.6 fen/kWh in 1995 prices.

5.10   TEPGC's Board of Directors decided to staff and operate the proposed power
plant according to international standards and best commercial practices to minimize
operating expenses. TEPGC is therefore expected to have a low operating ratio, which
will not exceed 48.2 percent during its first eight years of operation (2001-08). Its
average rates of return on historically valued and revalued net fixed assets, projected at
8.8  and  6.9  percent respectively, are  also  satisfactory.   However, debt service
requirements would be substantial. Specifically, debt service is forecast to account for an
average of 66 percent of TEPGC's operating income during the first eight years of
operation.
5.11    Funds Flow. During the project implementation period (1996-2002), TEPGC's
financing plan would be dominated by the proposed project. After commissioning of the
project, TEPGC is expected to generate substantial internal cash. As NCPGC would be
the sole customer and is expected to maintain a sound liquidity position (para. 5.22), no
problem is envisaged regarding collection of payments. However, as noted above, the
corporation would have a heavy debt service burden. The projected minimum tariff
requirements are mainly determined by the agreed debt service coverage ratio during
2003-08.
5.12    Capital Structure.  As required by the rule recently set by SPC  for all new
companies, three sponsors will invest equity shares equivalent to 25 percent of the project
investment cost in TEPGC. Within the first eight years of project operation, TEPGC's
capital structure is expected to improve considerably through retention of earnings and



- 41 -
loan repayments. During the same period, its debt/equity ratio is forecast to improve
from a peak of 71/29 in 2001 to 43/57 in the year 2008.

sheets for the period 1993-95 are set out in Annex 5.3.

Debt Service Coverage                 4.2       2.9      3.9
Debt as % of Debt and Equity (%)     52.8      50.5     56.7
La Excluding VAT and other taxes.
5.14 NCPGC's financial performance in the past was generally satisfactory. During
the period 1993-95, NCPGC reported very modest profits with both net profitability on
equity and assets basis in the range of 2 to 3 percent. NCPGC's operating revenue
increased by 41 percent from 1993 to 1995 due to higher average tariff and energy sales.
However, its operating expenses increased even faster at a rate of 43 percent over the
same period as a result of over 60 percent increase of all operating expenses, except fuel
expenditures (8.5 percent). NCPGC has maintained a relatively conservative capital
structure with debt not exceeding 60 percent of total debt and equity over years. Its
liquidity position has been rather comfortable at levels over 1.5 in the past three years.
However, NCPGC's current tariff level is below the long-run marginal cost.
Financial Performance Targets
5.15  To promote prudent and sound financial management and adequate tariffs,
assurances were obtainedfrom NCPGC that it would:



- 42 -
(a)   take all necessary measures, including but not limited to tariff
adjustments, to ensure its internal cash generation would provide a debt
service coverage ratio of no less than 1.5 times at all times;
(b)   take all necessary measures, including but not limited to tariff
adjustments, to earn an annual return on equity of not less than 8 percent
in 1998, 10 percent in 1999-2000, and 12 percent thereafter, and
(c)   by April 30 of each year, furnish to the Bank a rolling eight-year financial
plan containing projected income statements, statements of sources and
uses offunds, and balance sheets.
5.16  Achievement of these objectives will require substantial tariff increases.
Projections, presented in Table 5.3, show that NCPGC's average price must increase by a
minimum of about 16.7 percent (over 9.2 percent in real terms) per year from 1995 to
2001 so as to achieve a rate of return on equity of 12 percent.
Future Finances
5.17  The projections of NCPGC's finances for 1996-2003 are presented in Annex 5.4,
and the salient features of these finances are highlighted below in Table 5.3. The
projections are based on the assumptions contained in Annex 5.5.
5.18  Table 5.3 shows the projected price increases of purchased power, NCPGC's self-
generated power, and the weighted average of those two that will be required to meet the
financial covenants. The prices for purchased power will be determined on a plant-by-
plant basis, at "debt service price" levels, allowing for full debt service based on a seven
to eight-year payback period after commissioning of new power plants.
5.19  NCPGC's financial position, however, depends mostly on revenues that it collects
from its self-generated power. In order to meet the expansion plan and projected cost
increases in the future and comply with the financial performance targets set forth above,
NCPGC' s average sale price will need to increase by an estimated 15.2 percent in current
terms or 7.9 percent in real terms a year from 1995 to 2003. Given the current low
catalog prices, 20.4 fen/kWh in 1995, the average price of NCPGC's self-generated
power needs to triple over the period.
5.20  Although the rates of increase in the catalog prices needed for NCPGC are large,
much of the increase should stem from the entry of new generating plant into the rate
base. Because of the rapid load growth, the bulk of the tariff increase required will be
met through implementation of the new pricing principles. At the end of 1996, more than
half of NCPGC's own generation came from plants built since 1993.

5.21   During the period 1996-2003, NCPGC needs to maintain relatively high rates of
growth to meet the electricity demand required by the sustained strong economic growth
in the North China region. Based on the latest demand forecast and expansion plan
assumptions, energy sales are expected to rise by 85.1 percent over 1995 levels,
representing an average annual growth rate of about 8 percent. NCPGC's annual
investment program and net fixed assets in operation are expected to increase by 5.0- and
4.5-fold over the period, an annual increase of about 25 and 24 percent, respectively.
5.22   From 1996 on, NCPGC's performance is anticipated to improve gradually and
steadily as the tariff level of NCPGC's own generation is increased to meet the financial
targets, from 23 fen/kWh in 1995 to 83.3 fen/kWh in 2003 and tariff structure is further
rationalized. The debt service requirement emerges as an important determinant of future
price levels. Based on the projected minimum tariff level, about 17.5 percent increase per
year for its own generation and 10.8 percent for purchased power from 1995 to 2003,
both revenues and net income are expected to grow considerably over the period.
NCPGC is also expected to maintain a sound liquidity position with an average current
ratio of 1.42 over the projection period. The rate of return on historically valued assets
and on revalued assets will improve considerably during 1996-2003.
D. AUDIT
5.23   Under the proposed project, both TEPGC and NCPGC's accounts will be audited
by the Foreign Investment Audit Bureau of the State Audit Administration (SAA). This
arrangement is satisfactory. Assurances were obtained from TEPGC and NCPGC that



- 44 -
they would (a) maintain and provide to the Bank semiannual progress reports with
unaudited project accounts to reflect project expenditures for the period under report
compared with the original project cost estimates; (b) furnish the Bank with the audited
accounts for the project, statements of expenditures, andfinancial statements of TEPGC
and NCPGC within six months of the end of each fiscal year; (c) implement any changes
to its accounting practices as recommended by their financial management consultants to
meet international accounting standards; and (d) require their auditors to comply with
international auditing practices and provide audit plans prior to each audit. For the first
audit report, the auditors are expected to provide: (a) a summary of the effects of
accounting changes arising from the adoption of new MOF regulations and corrections of
any deviations from MOF regulations as interpreted with reference to international
accounting standards and practices; and (b) a management letter summarizing any
significant matters that may have arisen during the conduct of the audit.

6. PROJECT JUSTIFICATION AND RISK
A. POWER PLANT
Least Cost Analysis
6.1   The Jing-Jin-Tang area is very important and fast growing industrial and
commercial base in China. However, it has limited electricity generation potential with
very limited hydroelectric and coal resources. During the last decade, it relied mostly on
imported coal from Shanxi Province and Inner Mongolia for its power generation.
However, because of environmental concerns, scarcity of adequate generation sites and
overload of the railway system, NCPGC started importing power from mine-mouth
power plants in Inner Mongolia and Shanxi Province to meet the surging demand in the
Jing-Jin-Tang area.
6.2   During the preparation of the project, a least-cost expansion study was carried out
by the Beijing Economic Research Institute for Water Resources and Electric Power
(referred to as BERI), covering 1998-2025 (study period) with a focus on 1998-2010
(planning period).  The study was carried out using an optimization model that
determines the optimal capacity and generation mix to meet the demand with the
minimum cost (net present value of investment and operating costs), given a
predetermined reliability criteria. It considered all potential candidates including: power
imports from different coal-fired power plants in Inner Mongolia and Shanxi Province
(300 and 600 MW units), 300 MW distillate fueled combined cycle units, 300 and 600
MW coal-fired units in the Jing-Jin-Tang area, hydropower and pumped storage plants,
and gas turbines (100 MW units). Considering economic costs for all candidates
(including transmission costs in case of electricity transfer) and a discount rate of 12
percent, the study showed that the expansion program should, up to the year 2010, be
based mostly on 300 MW and 600 MW subcritical coal-fired units (preferably in Inner
Mongolia Autonomous Region or Shanxi Province) for base load and gas turbines and
pumped storage plants for peak load 2 The proposed project is the first investment of the
optimal (least-cost) power development program.
6.3   Sensitivity analyses confirmed the robustness of the proposed project, which
remains the first investment of the expansion program under a wide range of
assumptions:
2 Introduction of this highly automated and sophisticated technology in the Inner Mongolia Autonomous
Region is considered an unacceptable risk by the Beneficiary (and the Chinese Government). However,
the least-cost study showed that supercritical units are less economically attractive because of the low
economic coal price in Inner Mongolia and Shanxi.



- 46 -
(a)   The project will still be required by year 2001-02, even if the demand
growth would slow from the projected 9 percent per year to only 6 percent
during 1995-2000, and from about 8 percent per year to 5.5 percent during
2000-2005;
(b)   The project would be less economical than an equivalent combined cycle
power plant only if the cost of distillate were more than 70 percent lower
than in the base case ($40 per ton of coal equivalent, $1.5 per MBtu,
compared to $141 per ton of coal equivalent, $5.2 per MBtu). Distillate
has been considered because of nonavailability of gas in North China in
the foreseeable future; and
(c)   The project remains the first investment of the least-cost program and is
still needed in 2001-02 even if the discount rate is increased from 12 to 14
percent.
6.4   The balances between power demand and supply are presented in Annex 6.1.
They demonstrate that the power system will be short of energy supply up to the year
2005.
Economic Rate of Return
6.5   The internal economic rate of return (IERR) for the proposed project was
calculated based on the estimated plant gate power price of 33 fen/kWh (in 1995 prices)
in 2001, the first year of operation. This estimate is based on prices paid to the newest
baseload power plants selling power to NCPGC in 1995, according to existing policies
for pricing of power output from new power plants. It yielded an IERR of about 18.9
percent. This IERR, based on revenues generated by the new power plant rather than the
economic value of a shortfall in electric power supply, underestimates the economic
benefits of the project.
6.6   The IERR would decrease to the test discount rate of 12 percent, in the case of
one of the following occurrences:
(a)   the price paid at the plant gate for electricity were about 28 percent lower
than the base case price: i.e., 24 fen/kWh instead of 33 fen/kWh in 1995
prices;
(b)   the project were to experience a cost overrun of about 55 percent;
(c)   the power plant operation were reduced to about 4,200 hours per year
instead of about 6,000 hours;
(d)   the economic fuel cost were 140 percent higher than the base case;

6.7   However, these occurrences are very unlikely because China has a very good
record in project implementation, the economic assumptions considered in the base case
are conservative, and the power purchase agreement and the financial covenants of the
project would ensure that prices are high enough to meet the economic viability
requirements.
Environmental Benefits
6.8   BERI also conducted an economic evaluation of the environmental impacts of the
project based on the "Benefits Transfer Method." It used: (a) the New York Externality
Model (Rowe et al., 1994) to determine adjusted estimate of the monetary damages
caused by air pollution during the study period, taking into account the location of the
power plant, the emission level and the population affected, and (b) estimates of average
annual climate change for carbon emissions to evaluate the monetary damages caused by
greenhouse gas emissions.
6.9   The results of the study showed that if the economic cost of the environmental
impacts is added to the project costs, the IERR would be reduced by 2.3 percent, 16.6
percent instead of 18.9 percent in the base case. It also confirmed that the economic cost
of the environmental impacts of the proposed project is lower than the economic cost of
the environmental impacts of a comparable power plant in the Jing-Jin-Tang area. If the
cost differential is added to the benefits of the proposed project, the IERR would increase
to 19.7 percent.
Project Risk
6.10  To complement the above deterministic analysis, risk analyses have been
performed using a probabilistic approach to assess the impact of the perceived risk factors
that affect the return of the project.
6.11  Four broad categories of risk have been considered in the analysis:
(a)   Demand Risk. Demand risk is related to lower load growth than expected
and/or breach of contract by the purchaser. Demand risk is not perceived
as a major risk factor in the short term with energy shortages prevailing in
the Jing-Jin-Tang grid. However, a low-load growth and/or an important
reduction in electricity off take could jeopardize the economic viability of
the project.
(b)   Price Risk. Price risk is related to the willingness and ability of the power
purchaser to pay and/or changes in pricing policy. This risk is normally
mitigated or managed through long-term contractual arrangements.



- 49 -
However, experience shows that the seller could still be confronted to
unexpected price changes.
(c)    Cost Risk.  Higher-than-estimated project cost usually results from:
(i)higher equipment prices.  The major equipment and facilities of
generation will be purchased through international competitive bidding.
Prices could therefore be affected by market fluctuations; (ii) construction
cost overrun. Cost overrun during construction period is a common
phenomenon for many infrastructure projects. They are caused by a
number of factors, in particular, delay of construction due to interfacing
problems between different suppliers, unforeseen site conditions and
inadequate construction management; and (iii) coal prices. Coal prices
increase would result in a major operating cost increase of the power plant.
It is likely that the coal price would stay stable in the initial years.
However, it is possible that at later stages of the project lifetime there
might be a coal price increase due to more stringent environmental
constraints (higher price for better quality coal, requirement to wash the
lower quality coal, etc.).
(d)    Operational Performance Risk. Poor operational performance could be
caused by:  (i) low  quality of generating equipment and facilities,
(ii) below standard coal, and (iii) inadequate maintenance and poor
management.
6.12   Based on the foregoing considerations and extensive sensitivity analyses, five
variables have been selected as the crucial risk variables because of their significant
impact on the project economic viability. Table 6.2 presents the selected variables, the
assumed value ranges and the probability distributions attached to them based on past
Bank experience in power projects in general and China in particular.
6.13   The risk analysis was carried out with the Risk Master computer software, which
uses Monte Carlo simulation3 technique. The spreadsheet employed for the risk analysis
is based on the base case cost/benefit analysis (see Table 6.1). The correlation between
risk variables is explicitly considered prior to the simulation to avoid generation of
unrealistic project scenarios. For instance, variations of output and cost are correlated
with the construction lead time.
6.14 The results, based on 1,000 simulations, are summarized in Table 6.3 and Figure
6.1.
3 Monte Carlo simulation works by generating a series of random numbers following the distribution of
probability of each risk variable. For each simulation which represents a combination of different state
of 5 variables, IERR is calculated and recorded. The results of 1,000 simulations are averaged.

6.15   The expected IERR, based on the weighted average of all simulated combinations
is 17.91 percent (with a standard deviation of 2.40 percent), 1 percent lower than the base
case value without consideration of project risks. The minimum and maximum IERR,
under the considered uncertainties, are respectively 11.03 and 26.24 percent. The
probability of a negative outcome (negative net present value) is zero. And finally, the
probability for the IERR to be below the considered discount rate is negligible.
6.16   All the above analyses clearly show that the project is robust under future
uncertainties and that the risk (margin of error) associated with it is acceptable. The
potential risks associated with power plant construction, cost overruns, and
implementation delays have been addressed through advanced procurement actions,
timely staffing and training of personnel of TEPGC, and the assistance provided by
highly experienced international engineering consultants.



- 52 -
B. THE TRANSMISSION AND DISTRIBUTION COMPONENT
6.17  Operation records and load flow/short circuit analyses of the Beijing transmission
and distribution networks show that a number of 220 kV and 110 kV substations and
lines were overloaded and/or exceeding their rated fault levels in supplying the existing
demand. This causes a major safety and reliability concern in operating the entire North
China Power Grid. In January 1995, Beijing experienced a major blackout due to limited
transformation capacity in existing substations and limitation of the load transit capacity.
In the area where the new 220 kV substations are proposed, three out of five existing
110 kV substations were loaded at 92, 98 and 104 percent of their respective rated
capacity in 1995. The existing system is saturated and require major reinforcements to
adequately service the current load and supply any additional demand.
6.18  The proposed transmission upgrading plans were selected after thorough technical
analyses by NCEPDI and NCPGC. They represent the minimum requirements to avoid
further deterioration and ensure supply with acceptable standards of quality. A least-cost
analysis of this component was undertaken by BERI. Two major alternatives that are the
only feasible supply options were investigated:  (a) the first alternative (proposed
component) is based on introduction of two more injection points at the 220 kV level
closer to the city center (the Xibianmen and Xizhimen substations) to supply a number of
110 kV substations that are either existing or under construction, and (b) the second
alternative is based on continued expansion of the existing 110 kV transmission systems
by adding new 110 kV substations and lines. Considering the same incremental demand,
the cost stream for each alternative, over a project life of 30 years (economic investment
costs, operation and maintenance (O&M) costs as 2 percent of the capital investment
costs, and the estimated cost of losses based on the long-run marginal cost of supply), and
a 12 percent discount rate, the computed net present value of the costs of first alternative
is about $23 million, 17 percent, lower than that of the second alternative. This is a
conservative estimate because the cost of land and right-of-way, which is higher in the
second alternative, was not included.
6.19  Cost/benefit analysis of the component was carried out using the economic costs
and the incremental sales revenue as minimum benefits. The costs include: (a) capital
investments under the project, (b) investment costs at 110 kV and below to support the
demand growth, (c) the incremental O&M costs calculated at 2 percent of the capital
costs, (d) expenses for incremental power purchase (including 5 percent for power plant
use and 5 percent for transmission losses) valued at the system long-run marginal cost.
The power sales price considered for evaluating the incremental benefits is the current
average tariff and, therefore, does not exceed consumers' minimum willingness to pay.
The 15.55 percent IERR yielded by this methodology could be considered as a minimum
rate of return on the proposed transmission component.  The project remains
economically viable even in case of cost increases of 10 (IERR of 14.25 percent) and 20
(IERR of 13.10 percent) percent.



- 53 -
6.20 Upgrading of the distribution system in the heavily loaded, high-growth Xizhimen
area is a pilot project as part of a large-scale urban distribution rehabilitation program that
is being developed in association with the transmission development plan. At present,
about 60 percent of distribution lines are overloaded and 36 percent of installed
distribution transformers in Beijing are high-loss transformers. The annual losses were
about 220 GWh.  The technical aspect including loss reduction measures were
investigated by NCPGC and the economic justification was undertaken by BERI. The
major benefits are seen as the reduced losses and increased supply. The benefits of
reduced losses were calculated using the average consumer price of the Beijing
Municipality Grid at the retail level and the benefits of the incremental sales were
measured as the difference between the current average retail tariff and the average
transfer price at the high-voltage level. This conservative approach, which does not
include the benefit of improved quality of supply, yielded an IERR of 29 percent for the
pilot project. Sensitivity analysis showed that if the capital costs would increase by 30
percent, the IERR would still be 25 percent.
C. SUSTAINABILITY AND RISK OF THE PROJECT
6.21  Financial sustainability of the major project component is ensured by the PPA
developed by and signed between TEPGC and NCPGC prior to project negotiations and
suitable financial project covenants. The market for the power is guaranteed and NCPGC
will have no major problems in fulfilling its obligations under the PPA, even in the case
of moderate growth. Suitable performance monitoring indicators have been developed
that will allow impending major problems to be detected and corrective measures taken if
necessary.
6.22  All proposed technologies are currently in successful use in China. Cost overrun
risks have been addressed by advanced procurement action; bids for major components
have already been prepared before loan negotiations and issued prior to Board
presentation. The risk of delay in project construction due to project management
shortcomings is minimal because of the experience gained during the implementation of
the Haibowan and Dalate projects (two large coal-fired power plant in Inner Mongolia
Autonomous Region), the timely staffing and training of personnel of the TEPGC and
NCPGC, and the assistance provided by experienced international engineering
consultants. The risk of nonperformance in the environmental and resettlement area has
been addressed by the establishment of appropriate monitoring organizations and
procedures.  More importantly, sensitivity analyses show that even in case of
implementation of more stringent environmental standards requiring future installation of
flue gas desulfurization (FGD), the project would remain economically viable.
6.23  Delay in implementing power sector reforms in an efficient and timely manner
represents a risk that can jeopardize the progress toward more openness and efficiency of
the sector and more fiscal and managerial autonomy of TEPGC and NCPGC. This risk is
minimized by the strong commitment of Chinese institutions to reform both at the central
and provincial level. This risk is also addressed through provision of required technical



- 54 -
assistance and adequate monitoring of implementation of the agreed upon reform plan
during the project cycle.
6.24 The economic and financial risks would be minimal. In view of advanced
preparatory works, risks associated with project construction, cost overruns, and
implementation delays are within reasonable limits and manageable with the agreed
supervision and involvement of competent consultants. Particular attention will be given
to the safety aspects of the project, and to capability and performance of major
contractors.
6.25  Risk analyses have been performed using a probabilistic approach to assess the
impact of the perceived risk factors that affect the return of the project. The risk analysis
was carried out with the Risk Master computer software, which uses Monte Carlo
simulation technique. The expected IERR, based on the weighted average of 1,000
simulated combinations is 17.91 percent (with a standard deviation of 2.40 percent),
1.00 percent lower than the base case value without consideration of project risks. The
minimum and maximum IERR, under the considered uncertainties, are respectively 11.03
and 26.24 percent. The probability of a negative outcome (negative net present value) is
zero. And finally, the probability for the IERR to be below the considered discount rate
is negligible.

7. AGREEMENTS AND RECOMMENDATIONS
7.1   The following assurances were obtained at negotiations:
(a)   From the Borrower that it would ensure that the proceeds of the loan are
onlent to the Beneficiaries under subsidiary loan agreements on the same
terms and conditions as the Bank loan and that the Beneficiaries bear the
foreign exchange risk (para. 4.38);
(b)   From TEPGC that it would:
(i)   carry  out the  organizational restructuring  and  financial
management systems studies according to the schedule agreed with
the Bank; and implement the recommended organizational
improvements, and accounting and financial management systems,
taking into account Bank comments (para. 3.12);
(ii)   carry out the environment program in a manner satisfactory to the
Bank (para. 4.21);
(iii)   carry out the resettlement program for the Tuoketuo Thermal
Power Plant and ash disposal site in accordance with the agreed
resettlement plan, and prepare a RAP for the railway connection
and cooling water supply system by September 30, 1997 and carry
out the resettlement program as agreed with the Bank (para. 4.24);
(iv)    carry out the management development and training program as
agreed with the Bank (para. 4.31);
(v)   take all necessary measures, including but not limited to tariff
adjustments, to earn an annual return of not less than 20 percent of
the equity (defined in this case as the total unimpaired paid-in
capital) in 2003 and thereafter (para. 5.7);
(vi)   take all necessary measures, including but not limited to tariff
adjustments, to ensure its internal cash generation would provide a
debt service coverage ratio of no less than 1.3 times during
2001-03, and 1.5 times thereafter (para. 5.7);
(vii)   furnish to the Bank, by April 30 of each year, a rolling eight-year
financial plan containing projected income statements, funds flow
statements, and balance sheets (para. 5.7);
